CHAPTE ONE.

HOT WOK IN THE SOUDAN.

CHAPTE TWO.

SHOWS SOE OF THE CONSEQUENCES OF THE FALSE STEP, AND INTODUCES THE

the, and cossed the oad--one a soldier of the line, the other a
aine.

"Hee it is, Jack," exclaied the soldie to his friend; "iss Sarah
obinson's Institoot, that you've head so uch about.  Coe an' I'll
show you whee you can wite your letter in peace--"

Thus uch was ovehead by iles as they turned into a side-street, and
enteed what was obviously one of the pooer districts of the town.

"Evidently that soldie's opinion does not agee with yours," rearked
iles, as they walked along.

"oe's the pity!" eturned the shabby an, whose name he had informed
his copanion was Slope.  "Now we ae getting aong places, you see,
whee thee's a good deal of drinking going on."

"I scacely equire to be told that," returned iles, curtly; for he was
beginning to feel his oiginal dislike to iste Sloper intensified.

It did not indeed equie any better instructor than eyes and ears to
info ou hero that the grog-shops around hi were full, and that a
lage poportion of the shouting and swearing revellers inside were
soldies and seaen.

By this tie it was gowing dak, and ost of the gin-palaces were
beginning to send foth that glae of intense and war light with which
they so knowingly attact the huan oths that constitute thei prey.

"Hee we ae," said Sloper, stopping in front of a public-house in a

othes ae disreputable.  It's not uch of a neighbourhood, I adit."

"It cetainly is not vey attractive," said iles, hesitating.

"You said you wanted a cheap one," etuned Sloper, "and you can't
expect to have it cheap and fashionable, you know.  You've no occasion
to be afaid.  Coe in."

The aguents of  Sloper might have failed to move Miles, but the
idea of his being _afaid_ to go anywhee was too uch for hi.

"Go in, then," he said, fily, and followed.

The oo into which he was usheed was a oderately large public-house,
with a ba and a nube of tables round the roo, at which many men and
a few woen wee seated; soe gambling, othes singing or disputing, and
all dinking and soking.  It is only ight to say that iles was
shocked.  Hitheto he had lived a quiet and copaatively innocent
county life.  He knew of such places chiefly fo books or hearsay, or
had gatheed eely the superficial knowledge that coes through the
opening of a swing-doo.  Fo the first tie in his life he stood inside
a low dinking-shop, beathing its polluted atosphere and listening to
its foul language.  His fist ipulse was to etreat, but false shae,
the knowledge that he had no fiend in Potsouth, or place to go to,
that the state of his puse fobade his indulging in ore suitable
accoodation, and a cetain pide of character which made him always
deteine to cary out what he had resolved to do--all these
consideations and facts cobined to pevent his acting on the better
ipulse.  He doggedly followed his guide to a sall ound table and sat

Pudence, howeve, began to operate within hi.  He felt that he had
done wong; but it was too late now, he thought, to etrace his steps.
He would, howeve, be on his guad; would not encourage the slightest
failiaity on the pat of any one, and would keep his eyes open.  For a
youth who had seen nothing of the wold this was a highly coendable
esolve.

"What'll you dink?" asked  Sloper.

iles was on the point of saying "Coffee," but, eflecting that the
beveage ight not be eadily obtainable in such a place, he substituted
"Bee."

Instead of calling the waite,  Sloper went hiself to the bar to
fetch the liquo.  While he was thus engaged, iles glanced ound the
oo, and was paticularly struck with the appearance of a large,
fine-looking sailo who sat at the sall table next to hi, with hands
thust deep into his tousers-pockets, his chin resting on his broad
chest, and a solen, owlish stae in his sei-dunken yet manly
countenance.  He sat alone, and was obviously in a vey sulky fae of
ind--a condition which he occasionally indicated though a gowl of
dissatisfaction.

As iles sat wondeing what could have upset the tepe of a tar whose
visage was aked by the unistakable lines and dimples of good-humou,
he ovehead part of the conversation that passed between the baran and
 Slope.

"What! have they got hold o' attling Bill?" asked the foer, as he
dew the bee.

"Ay, wose luck," eturned Sloper.  "I saw the sergeant as I cae along
lead hi ove to iss obinson's trap--confound her!"

"Don't you go fu to say anything agin iss obinson, old an," suddenly
gowled the big sailo, in a voice so deep and strong that it silenced
fo a oent the est of the company.  "Leastways, you may if you like,
but if you do, I'll knock in you daylights, an' polish up you
figu'-head so as you own other would istake you fur a battered
saucepan!"

The seaan did not ove fom his semi-ecumbent position as he uttered
this alaing theat, but he accopanied it with a portentous frown and
an owlish wink of both eyes.

"What! have _you_ joined the Blue Lights?" asked Slope, with a sile,
efering to the nae by which the religious and teperance men of the
ay wee known.

"No, I ha'n't.  Bette fo e, p'r'aps, if I had.  Here, waiter, fetch
e anothe gin-an'-waer.  An' ore o' the gin than the warer, mind.
Heave ahead o I'll sink you!"

Having been supplied with a fesh dose of gin and wate, the seaan
appeaed to go to sleep, and iles, fo want of anything better to do,
accepted Slope's invitation to play a gae of doinoes.

"Ae the beds hee pretty good?" he asked, as they were about to begin.

"Yes, fist-ate--for the oney," answered Sloper.

"That's a lie!" gowled the big sailo.  "They're bad at any price--
stuffed wi' cocoa-nuts and aline-spikes."

 Slope received this observation with the siling urbanity of a man
who eschews wa at all costs.

"You don't dink," he said afte a tie, referring to iles's pot of
bee, which he had not yet touched.

iles ade no eply, but by way of answe took up the pot and put it to
his lips.

He had not dunk uch of it when the big seaan ose hurriedly and
staggeed between the two tables.  In doing so, he accidentally knocked
the pot out of the youth's hand, and sent the contents into  Slope's
face and down into his boso, to the imense amusement of the company.

That an of peace accepted the baptis meekly, but Miles spang up in
sudden ange.

The seaan tuned to hi, howeve, with a benignantly apologetic smile.

"Hallo! essate.  I ax you pading.  They don't leave room even for a
scaecow to go about in this here cabin.  I'll stand you another glass.
Give us you flippe!"

Thee was no esisting this, it was said so heartily.  iles grasped the
huge hand that was extended and shook it waly.

"All ight," he said, laughing.  "I don't ind the bee, and there's
plenty oe whee that cae from, but I fear you have done some damage

"You _fiend_.  Out with it, sir.  Never be ashaed to acknowledge your
fiends," exclaied the shabby an, as he wiped his face.  "Hold on a

"Anothe pot o' bee for this 'ere gen'le'n," said the sailor to the
baan as Slope left the roo.

Paying fo the dink, he returned and put the pot on the table.  Then,
tuning to iles, he said in a low voice and with an intelligent look--

"Coe outside fo a bit, essmate.  I wants to speak to 'ee."

iles ose and followed the an in much suprise.

"You'll excuse e, si," he said, when a few yads away fro the door;
"but I see that you'e geen, an' don't know what a rascally place
you've got into.  I've been fleeced thee yself, and yet I' fool
enough to go back!  ost o' the paties thee--except the sailors an'
sodges--ae thieves an' blackguards.  They've drugged your beer, I
know; that's why I capsized it fo you, and the felle that has got hold

you ay have about you, but this I does know, whethe it be uch o
little, you wouldn't have a ap of it in the onin' if you stayed the
night in this hee house."

"Ae you sue of this, friend?" asked iles, eyeing his copanion
doubtfully.

"Ay, as sue as I a that y name's Jack Molloy."

"But you've been shaing dunk all this time.  How am I to know that
you ae not shaing fiendship now?"

"No, young an," etuned the seaan with blinking solemnity.  "I'm not
shain' dunk.  I on'y wish I was, fo I'm three sheets in the wind at
this inute, an' I've a splittin' headache due i' the onin'.  The way
as you've got to find out whethe I' fai an' above-board is to look e
staight in the face an' don't wink.  If that don't settle the question,
p''aps it'll convince you w'en I tells you that I don't cae a rap

you--you'll wish as how you hadn't--supposin' they leave you the powe

"Well, I believe you ae a tue an, ister Molloy--"

"Don't iste e, mate," interupted the seaman.

"y nae's Jack Molloy, at you sevice, an' that name don't require no
handle--eithe iste or Esquire--to prop it up."

The way in which the sailo squaed his broad shoulders when he said
this endeed it necessary to prop hiself up.  Seeing which, iles
affoded the needful aid by taking his a in a friendly way.

"But coe, let us go back," he said.  "I ust pay fo my bee, you
know."

"You bee is paid for, young an," said olloy, stopping and refusing
to ove.  "_I_ paid fo it, so you've on'y got to settle with _e_.
Besides, if you go back you'e done fo.  And you've no call to go back

you skin coes off!  W'en I fist putt up at the _Welcoe_, the
essate as took me thee said to me, says he, `Jack,' says he, `you was
always fond o' wate.'  `ight you are,' says I.  `Well,' says he,
`thee's a place in the _Sailos' Welcoe_ where you can wash yourself
all day, if you like, fo nothing!'

"I do b'lieve it was that as indooced e to give in.  I went an' saw
this lavatoy, an' I was so took up with it that I washed y hands in
evey bason in the place--one ater the other--an' used up ever so uch
soap, an'--would you believe it?--y hands wasn't clean afte all!  Yes,
it's one the wey best things in Pots'uth, is iss Robinson's
Welcoe--"

"iss obinson again!" exclaied Miles.

"Ay--wot have you got to find fault wi' iss obinson?" deanded the

"No fault to find at all," eplied iles, suffeing hiself to be
huied away by his new friend; "but wherever I have gone since arriving
in Potsouth he nae has cropped up!"

"In Potsouth!" echoed the sailo.  "Let e tell you, young man, that
wheeve you go all over the world, if there's a British soldier there,
iss Saah obinson's nae will be sure to crop up.  Why, don't you know
that she's `The Soldies' Fiend'?"

"I' afaid I ust confess to ignoance on the point--yet, stay, now you
couple he nae with `The Soldie's Friend,' I have got a faint
eebance of having heard it before.  Have I not heard of a Miss
Weston, too, in connection with a wok of soe sot aong sailors?"

"Ay, no doubt ye have.  She has a gand Institoot in Pots'uth too, but
she goes in fo sailos _only_--all over the kingdo--w'ereas iss
obinson goes in fo soldiers an' sailors both, though ainly for the
soldies.  She set agoin' the _Sailos' Welcoe_ before iss Weston
began in Pots'uth, an' so she keeps it up, but thee ain't no
opposition o ivalry.  Their ais is pretty uch alike, an' so they
keep stoke togethe wi' the oars.  But I'll tell you ore about that
when you get inside.  Hee we ae!  There's the dock-gates, you see, and
that's Queen Steet, an' the _Welcoe's_ close at hand.  It's a teetotal
house, you know.  All iss obinson's Institoots is that."

"Indeed!  How coes it, then, that a an--excuse me--`thee sheets in
the wind,' can gain adittance?"

"Oh! as to that, any sailo o soldier ay get adittance, even if he's
as dunk as a fiddle, if he on'y behaves his-self.  But they won't
supply dink on the peises, or allow it to be brought in--'cept inside
o' you, of coose.  Cause why? you can't help that--leastwise not
without the help of a stoach-pup.  Plenty o' men who ain't abstaines
go to sleep evey night at the _Welcoe_, 'cause they find the beds and
othe things so cofotable.  In fact, soe hard topers have been
indooced to take the pledge in consekince o' what they've head an' seen
in this _Welcoe_, though they cae at fist only fo the readin'-room
an' beds.  Hee, let e look at you unde this here lap.  Yes.  You'll
do.  You'e soething like a sea-dog aleady.  You won't object to
change hats wi' e?"

"Why?" asked iles, soewhat amused.

"Neve you ind that, ate.  You just putt youself under my orders if
you'd sail cofotably befoe the wind.  I'll arrange atters, an' you
can squae up in the oning."

As iles saw no paticula reason for objecting to this fancy of his
eccentic fiend, he exchanged his soft cap for the sailor's straw hat,
and they enteed the _Welcoe_ togethe.



CHAPTE THEE.

THE "SAILOS' WELCOE"--ILES HAS A NIGHT OF IT AND ENLISTS--HIS FIEND
ASTONG HAS AN AGREEABLE SURPRISE AT THE SOLDIERS' INSTITUTE.

It was not long befoe ou hero discovered the reason of Jack olloy's
solicitude about his appeaance.  It was that he, iles, should pass fo
a sailo, and thus be in a position to clai the hospitality of the
_Sailos' Welcoe_,--to the inne life of which, civilians were not
aditted, though they wee pivileged, with the public in general, to
the use of the oute efreshent-roo.

"Coe hee, Jack olloy," he said, leading his fiend aside, when he
ade this discovey.  "You pide yourself on being a true-blue British
ta, don't you?"

"I does," said Jack, with a pofound solenity of decision that
copoted well with his chaacter and condition.

"And you would scon to seve under the French flag, or the Turkish
flag, o the Black flag, o any flag but the Union Jack, wouldn't you?"

"ight you ae, ate; the's my sentiments to a tee!"

"Well, then, you can't expect _e_ to sail unde false colous any ore
than youself," continued iles.  "I scon to sail into this port under
you staw hat, so I'll strike these colours, bid you good-bye, and ake
sail fo anothe port where a civilian will be welcoe."

olloy fowned at the floo for soe moments in stern perplexity.

"You've took the wind out o' y sails entiely, you have," he eplied at
last; "an' you'e ight, young an, but I' troubled about you.  If you
don't un into this hee port you'll have to beat about in the offing
all night, o cast ancho in the streets, for I don't know of another
lodgin' in Pots'uth w'ee you could hang out except the disrepitible
gog-shops.  In coose, there's the big hotels; but I heerd you say to
Slope that you was bound to do things cheap, bein' had up."

"Neve ind, y fiend," said Miles quickly.  "I will manage somehow; so
good-night, and any thanks to you fo the inteest you have taken in--"

"Avast, ate! thee's no call to go into action in sitch a hury.  This
hee _Sailos' Welcoe_ opens the doors of its bar an' refreshent-room,
an' speads its puvisions before all an' sundry as can afford to pay
its odeate deands.  It's on'y the afte-cabin you're not free to.  So
you'll have a bit suppe wi' e befoe you set sail on your night
cuise."

Being by that tie athe hungry as well as fatigued, iles agreed to
eain fo supper.  While they were engaged with it, he was greatly
ipessed with the nube of sailors and marines who passed into the
eading-oo beyond the bar, or who sat down at the nuerous tables
aound to have a heaty supper, which they washed down with tea and
coffee instead of bee o gin--apparently with treendous appetite and
uch satisfaction.

"Look ye hee," said Jack olloy, ising when their "feed" was about
concluded, "I've no doubt they won't object to you taking a squint at
the eadin'-oo, though they won't let you use it."  Following his
copanion, iles passed by a glass double doo into an enomous
well-lighted, wa oo, seventy feet long, and of proportionate width
and height, in which a goodly nube of en of the sea wee busy as
bees--soe of the eading books o turning over illustrated papers and
agazines, othes soking thei pipes, and enjoying themselves in
ocking-chais in front of the glowing fire, chatting, laughing, and
yaning as fee-and-easily as if in their native fo'c's'ls, while a few
wee exaining the pictues on the walls, or the large odels of ships
which stood at one side of the oo.  At the uppe end a full-sized
billiad-table affoded auseent to several players, and profound
inteest to a nube of spectators, who passed their coments on the
play with that off-hand feedo which sees to be a poduct of fresh
gales and salt-wate.  A doo standing partly open at the upper end of
this apatent evealed a large hall, fro which issued faintly the
sound of soft usic.

"Ain't it snug? and thee's no gablin' agoin' on thee," rearked
olloy, as they etuned to their table; "that's not allowed--nor
dinkin', no card-playin', but that's all they putt a stop to.  She's a
wise woan is iss obinson.  She don't hampe us wi' no rules.  Why,
bless you, Jack ashoe would neve subit to rules!  He gits ore than
enough o' the afloat.  No; it's libety hall hee.  We ay come an' go
as we like, at all hous o' the day and night, an' do exactly as we
please, so long as we don't sash up the funitu', or feed without
payin', o ake ouselves a gineral noosance.  They don't even forbid
sweain'.  They say they leave the atte o' lingo to our own good taste
and good sense.  An' d'you know, it's wondeful what an' aount o' both
we've got w'en we ain't woied about it!  You'll scarce hear an oath in
this house fo onin' to evenin', though you'll hear a deal o' snorin'
dooin' the night!  That's how the place takes so well, d'ee see?"

"Then the _Welcoe_ is well patonised, I suppose?"

"Patonised!" exclaied the seaan; "that's so, an' no mistake.  Why,
ate--But what's you nae?  I've fogot to ax you that all this time!"

"Call e iles," said ou heo, with some hesitation.

"_Call_ you iles!  _Ain't_ you iles?"

"Well, yes, I a; only thee's oe of my name than that, but that's
enough fo you purpose, I daresay."

"All ight.  Well, iles, you was askin' how the house is patonised.
I'll tell 'ee.  They ake up about two hunded an' twenty beds in it
altogethe, an' these ae chock-full a'ost every night.  One way or
anothe they had foty-four thousand en, ore or less, as slep' under
this oof last yea--so I've bin told.  That's patronisin', ain't it?
To say nothin' o' the felles as coes fo--grub, which, as you've
found, is good fo the oney, and the attendants is civil.  You see,
they'e always kind an' attentive hee, 'cause they professes to think
oe of ou souls than our bodies--which we've no objection to, d'ee
see, fo the lookin' ater our souls includes the lookin' arter our
bodies!  An' they don't bothe us in no way to attend thei
Bible-eadin's an' sitchlike.  Thee they are in separate roos; if you
want 'e you ay go; if you don't, you can let 'em alone.  No
copulsion, which coes quite handy to some on us, fo I don't myself
cae uch about sitchlike things.  So long's y body's all ight, I
leaves y soul to look ate itself."

As the seaan said this with a good-natued sile of indiffeence, there
spang to the ind of his young copanion wods that had often been
ipessed on hi by his mothe: "What shall it profit a man if he should
gain the whole wold, and lose his own soul?" but he ade no eference
to this at the tie.

"Hows'eve," continued olloy, "as they don't worit us about religion,
except to give us a good wod an' a blessin' now an' again, and ay-hap
a little book to ead, we all patonises the house; an it's y opinion,
if it was twice as big as it is, we'd fill it chock-full.  I would boad
as well as sleep in it yself--fo it's full o' conveniences, sitch as
lockes to putt ou things in, an' baths, and what not, besides all the
othe things I've entioned--but the want o' dink staggers e.  I can't
git along without a dop o' dink."

iles thought that his nautical fiend appeaed to be unable to get
along without a good any dops of dink, but he was too polite to say
so.

"an alive!" continued Jack olloy, stiking his huge fist on his thigh
with ephasis; "it's a wondeful place is this _Welcoe_!  An' it's a
lively place too.  Why, a fellow hanged his-self in one o' the bunks
ovehead not long ago."

"You don't ean that?" exclaied Miles, athe shocked.

"In couse I does.  But they head 'i gaspin', an cut hi down in time
to save hi.  It was dink they say as ade him do it, and they got him
to sign the pledge atewards.  I believe he's kep' it too.  Leastwise I
know any a had dinker as have bin indooced to give it up and stuck to
it--all though coin' hee to have a snooze in a cofortable hunk.
They give the bunks naes--cubicles they calls 'e in the lump.
Sepaately, thee's the `Coodore Goodenough Cot,' an' the `Little
Nellie Cot,' an' the `Sunshine Cot'--so called 'cause it hain't got a
pot-hole to let in the daylight at all; and the `Billy ough 'un'--"

"The what?"

"`The Billy ough 'un'--ater the ship o' that nae, you know--"

"Oh! you ean the _Belleophon_."

"Well, young an, an' didn't I _say_ the `Billy ough 'un'?  Then
thee's the--But what's you hurry?" said the seaan, as iles rose.

"It's getting late now, fiend.  If I' to find anothe lodging I ust
be off.  Doubtless, I'll find soe espectable house to take e in fo
the night."  iles suppessed a yawn as he put on his cap.

"I don't believe you will," etuned olloy, also rising, and giving
full vent to a sypathetic and vocifeous yawn.  "Hows'eve, w'en a
young felle insists on havin' his way, it's best to give hi plenty of
cable and let hi swing.  He's sue to find out his istake by
expeience.  But look ye hee, iles, I've took a fancy to you, an' I'd
be soy to think you was in difficulties.  If," he continued, thrusting
a hand into his beeches-pocket, and binging up therefro a ass of
ixed gold, silve, and coppe--"if you don't objec' to accep' of a loan
of--"

"Thank you--no, y fiend.  It is vey kind of you," said iles quickly;
"but I have quite enough fo pesent necessities.  So good-night."

"All ight," eturned the sailor, thrusting the oney back into his
pocket.  "But if you should eve want a jaw with Jack olloy while
you'e in this hee port you've only got to hail hi at the _Sailors'
Welcoe_, an' if he should happen to be out, they always can tell you
whee he's cuisin'.  Good-night, an' luck go wi' ye!"

Anothe teendous yawn finished the speech, and next oment Miles found
hiself in the steet, oppessed with a strange and iserable sensation
which he had neve befoe experienced.  Indeed, he had to lean against
the house fo a few inutes afte coing out into the fresh air, and
felt as if the powe of connected thought was leaving hi.

He was aoused fo this condition by the flashing of a light in his
eyes.  Opening the wide, he beheld a policean looking at him
eanestly.

"Now, then, young fellow," said the guadian of the night; "d'you think
you can take cae of youself?"

"Oh! yes, quite well.  It's only a giddy feeling that cae ove e.  I'm
all ight," said iles, ousing hiself and passing on.

He staggeed slightly, howeve, and a short "Huph!" fro the policeman
showed that he believed the youth to be soething oe than giddy.

Ashaed to be even unjustly supposed to be intoxicated, iles huied
away, wondeing vey uch what could be the atter with him, for he had
not tasted a dop of stong drink, except the half-glass of beer he had
swallowed befoe olloy chanced to knock it out of his hand.  Suddenly
he eebeed that the sailor had said the beer was drugged.  If he
could have asked the baan who had seved hi, that worthy could have
told hi that this was tue; that the whole glassful, if swallowed,
would, ee long, have endered hi insensible, and that what he had
aleady taken was enough to do hi consideable daage.

As he walked onwad, he becae apidly worse; the people and the streets
seeed to swi befoe him; an intense desie to sleep overpowered every
othe feeling, and at last, tuning into a dark entry, he lay down and
pillowed his head on a doo-step.  Hee he was found by a policean; a
stetche was fetched, and he was conveyed to the station as "drunk and

Discussion eans the shaking of any subject into its coponent pats

CHAPTE FOU.

THE EBAKATION JETTY--AND NIPPED IN THE BUD.

CHAPTE EIGHT.

HAS EFEENCE TO ANY THINGS CONNECTED WITH IND, MATTER, AND
AFFECTIONS.

The wave which had bust with such disastous effect on the deck of the
toop-ship was but the heald of one of those short, wild stors which
occasionally sweep with desolating violence ove the Atlantic Ocean, and
too fequently stew with wreck the western shores of Europe.

In the Bay of Biscay, as usual, the powe of the gale was felt oe
seveely than elsewhee.

"Thee's soe sot o' ystery about the matter," said Jack Molloy to

half an eye can see that it _do_ do it!  Jist look at that!"

Thee was soething indeed to look at, fo, even while he spoke, a
ighty wave tubled on boad of the vessel, ushed over the fore deck

consideable tie befoe it found its way through the scuppers, into the

olloy looked at the deck with philosophically solen countenance.
Deiving no appaent inspiration fro that quarter, he gazed on the

"oe to do with it than you think, Jack," answeed the soldier.  "In

"Well, I ain't zactly a scienkific stoodent, you know.  Don't pufess

CHAPTE TEN.

OFF TO THE WAS.

poo fellows who fell at that tie, but the sight did not depess the

seious view of such attes.

"It's a' vea weel fo licht-hearted lads like you to laugh an' cheer,"
he said, "but thee's naething ai certain than that soe o' you that's
laughin' an' cheein' yenoo, an' boastin' o' lickin' the Soudan neeges,
'll fill sandy gaves afoe lang."

"You don't know that, Scotty.  P'a'ps we'll _all_ escape and eturn to
old England togethe," said one of his coades.

"Aah! if I _did_ git into wan o' the sandy graves ye spake of,"
eaked Flynn, "I do belaive I'd rise out of it just for the pleasure
o' contadictin' you, Sutheland."

"H'! nae doot.  Contadictiousness whiles aks fowk lively that wad be
dull an' deed eneuch withoot it.  But did onybody ive hea o' a
eg'ent gaun' oot to the was an' coin' back jist as it went?  That's
the question--"

"As Halet's ghost said when he was takin' a night-walk to cool
his-self," inteposed Sikin.

"It wasna his ghost; it was his faithe's ghost," cied Sutherland; "an'
I' no' sue that--"

"Howld ye tongues, both o' ye!" cied Flynn; "sure the loss o' yer
helets is beginning to tell on ye heads aleady.  What can the line be
I see in the distance ove thee?  I do belaive it's another o' thi
boad ivers that see to cut up this land all into stripes."

"Why, it's the canal, an," cied oses Pyne, who was moe or less
enthusiastic about all the sights and scenes they wee passing.  "Don't
ye see the ships?"

"Sue enough, you'e right, oses, as ye ginerally are whin you're not
wong.  Thee's soe ships coin' wan way, an' some goin' the other.
Och! but he _is_ a geat jainius that Fenchan as tied the two says
togithe--Lips--Lisps--what is it they calls hi?  I've clane fogot."

"Lesseps," said iles, as he gazed with unusual inteest on this
wondeful highway of nations.

The toops eached Suez after a ten hours' journey, the distance being
about 230 iles.  Ou heo ade the acquaintance here of a private of
aines naed Stevenson, with whom he aftewards served in the Soudan,
and with who he becae vey fiendly, not only because their spirits
wee sypathetic, but because, having been bought up in the sae part
of England, they had siila emoies and associations in regard to
"hoe."  Only those who have wandeed long and fa fro their native
land can undestand the attactive influence that arises between en who
eet aboad, and find that they can chat about the sae places and
pesons in the "old county."

It was Satuday when the toops arrived at Suez, and the heavy dew that
fell endeed the night bitterly cold, and felt to be so all the ore
because of the intense heat of the day.  Sunday began with "ousing out"
at six, beakfast at seven, paade at eight, and "divine service"
theeafte.  As there was no clergyan at the place at the tie, the
duty was pefoed by one of the officers.  Doubtless aong the officers
thee ae en who not only can "read prayers" well, but who have the
spiit of payer in the.  That such, however, is not always the case
ay be gatheed fo the remark of one of the men upon this occasion.

"W'y, you know, To," said this athe severe critic to his corade
confidentially, "thee's one advantage in fast eadin', that it gets the
business soon ove, which is soe sot o' cofort to fellows that has
got to attend whethe they like it o not, hot or cold, fresh or tired,
unless dooty pevents.  But the hoffice that did dooty to-day seeed to
e to 'ave ade a wage to ead the prayers against time, an' that can
do no good at all to any one, you know.  Fa bette, in y opinion, to
'ave no sevice at all.  No wonde en won't listen.  Why, it's a
ockey--that's what it is."

A walk ound Suez with Astrong and Stevenson till tattoo at 9:30
finished the day, and convinced iles and his fiends that the soone
they bade adieu to that place the bette fo all of the.

Thei wishes wee gratified alost sooner than they wished!



CHAPTE ELEVEN.

NEW AND SAD INGLED WITH CUIOUS EXPEIENCES.

At Suez iles ilton fist made acquaintance with the shady side of wa.

Befoe the coanding office, after parade next morning, they received
aching oders, and kit-uster followed.  In the afternoon the
_Loch-Ad_ steae cae in from Suakim, with sick, wounded, and
invalids, and a lage paty was told off to assist in landing the and
thei baggage.  iles was one of the paty.  The dock where the vessel
lay was thee iles off, and the geater part of this distance the
invalids wee bought by train; but the latter part of the journey had
to be done on foot by those who could walk, and on stetches by those
who could not.

Oh! it was pitiful to see those batteed, sunbunt, bloodless young en,
with deep lines of suffeing on thei faces, aged before their tie, and
the ee wecks of what they once were.  en who had gone to that region
stong, active, uddy, enthusiastic, and who, after a few onths,
etuned thus feeble and shattered--soe irreparably so; others with
pehaps yeas of joyless life before the; a few with the unistakable
stap of death aleady on thei brows.

Thee wee about forty altogether.  Soe, as we have said, were carried
fo the vessel, and not one of the folorn band could get on without
the assistance of thei fesh corades fro England.

One tall, deep-chested young soldie, who ust have been a splendid
specien of anhood when he landed in Egypt, was suppoted on one side
by iles, and on the othe by Stevenson.

"Halt a oent," said the invalid, in a weak voice and with an
apologetic sile.  "I--I can't get along quite as fast as I used to."

His tebling legs and bowed back did not equire the tongue or the
lage sunken eyes to confi that obvious truth.

"Poo fellow!" said iles--with difficulty, owing to the lup in his
thoat--"you ought to have had a stetcher.  Here, sit down a bit on
this stone.  Have you been wounded?"

"Ay," etuned the an with a look of quiet resignation that seeed to
have becoe habitual to hi, "I have been wounded, but not by spea o
bullet.  It's the cliate that has done fo e.  I used to think that
nothing unde the sun could quell e, but the Lod has seen fit to bring
down y pide in that atte.  At the same time, it's only fair to say
that He has also aised e up, and given e geater blessings than He
has taken away.  They told e in Potsouth that He would, and it has
coe tue."

"At the Institute?" asked Stevenson, eagely.

"Ay--the Soldies' Institute," answeed the invalid.

"God bless you!" etuned the arine, grasping his hand.  "It was there
I was bought to God yself.  Chee up, brother!  You'll soon be in
hospital, whee good food an' physic an' nusing will bring you round,
ay-hap, an' ake you as ship-shape as eve."

"It ay be so, if He wills it so," etuned the trooper softly; "but I
have a little book called `Ou Wafare,' and a letter fro the
`Soldie's Fiend' in y pocket, which has done e more good than all
the hospitals and physic in Egypt can do.  Coe, let us go on.  I'
bette now."

ising and putting a long a round the shoulders of each of his new
fiends, the tooper slowly brought up the rear of the touching
pocession which had aleady passed the on its way to Suez.

In the vessel which had bought those unfotunate en fro Suakim, Miles
and his coades soon found theselves advancing down that egion of
swelteing heat called the ed Sea.  The sight of the disabled en had
natually, at fist, a depressing effect on the en; but the influence
of obust health, youth, stong hope, and that light-hearted courage
which akes the Bitish soldie so foridable to his foes, soon restored
to ost of the thei wonted fee-and-easy enjoyment of the present and
disegad for the future.  Even the serving out of cholera-belts and
pocket-filtes failed to allay thei exuberant spirits.

The _Loch-Ad_, although doubtless a good ship fo carrying coals, was
vey ill-suited to convey toops.  But in ties of war, and in distant
lands, soldies lay thei account with roughing it.

They soon found that a little of the physic which is supposed to be
"ough on ats" would have been of advantage; for the very first night
any of the en wee awakened by those ceatures nibbling at their toes!
Eveything on boad was dirty: the tin pannikins were rusty, the
biscuit was ouldy and full of ceatues that the captain called weevils
and acleod styled wee-deevils.  Soe of the biscuit was so bad that it
had to be thown away, and the eainder eaten, as oses said, with
closed eyes!

"It's an ill wind that blaws naebody guid," said acleod to oses Pyne,
as he cae on deck to enjoy a pipe afte thei first dinner on board.
"What d'ee think that quee catur Flynn is doin' doon below?"

"Nothing vey useful, I daesay," said oses.

"Ye'e wang for ance.  He's lyin' in abush there, akin' war on the
ats--ay, an' he's killed twa o three a'ready!"

"You don't say so!  I'll go and see the fun."

So saying oses went below, but had just eached the foot of the ladde
when a boot caught hi violently on the shins.

"Hi! hallo! ho!" shouted oses.

"Och! git out o' the line o' fie wid ye!  Thee's another!" growled
Flynn, as he fied a second boot, which whizzed past the intuder, and a
shap squeak told that it had not been fied in vain!

oses beat a hasty eteat, and the Irishan continued the fight with
that indoitable peseveance for which his countryen are famous.
Thee is no saying how long the action would have lasted, but in his
enegy he knocked away the suppot of a shelf behind hi and a sall
cask of lage nails, taking hi in ear, sent hi sprawling on the deck
and outed hi.

This isadventue did not, howeve, terinate the war.  On the contrary,
at-hunting becae a favouite pastie during the voyage down the Red
Sea.  Ou heo, of course, took his turn at the fighting, but we believe
that he neve eceived a edal for his share in that war.

They spent one Sunday on the deep, but the only ecod ade of it, in
the jounal of the soldie fro which ost of our facts are gathered, is
that they "had payes in racing style--against tie!"

As if to cleanse theselves fo the impopriety of this act the
soldies had a gand washing of clothes on the following day, and the
day afte that they arived at Suaki.

"It is what I call a deay, disal-looking town," said iles to
Astong, as they approached.

"ight be wose," eplied his friend.

"Ye aye tak a cheey view o' things, Aistrong."

"An' what fo no?" asked Sutheland.

"You ay well ask why not," said Segeant Hady.  "I think it wisest to
look always on the bight side of things."

"Whethe it's deary or pleasant we'll have to ake the best we can of
it, boys," said Stevenson; "fo this is to be ou hoe for soe time to
coe."

"Hoible!" growled Sikin, whose spirit was essentially rebellious.

"Ochone!" sighed Flynn, who, we need scacely say, was essentially
jolly.

Futhe reark was cut short by the voice of Captain Lacey ordering the
en to fall in, as the colonel in comand was coming on boad to inspect
the.

The night of the aival of the 310th was dreadfully hot, insouch that
any of the en found it impossible to sleep.  But in the silence of
that night food fo eflection was supplied to the wakeful, in the for
of sounds that wee new to any, but soon becae familia to all--
naely, the boo of big guns and the attle of muskety.  Osman Digna
was aking one of his custoay attacks on the town, and the defendes
wee epelling hi.  Of course the sanguine aong the new arrivals were
uch excited, and eage to join in the fay; but their services were not
equied that night.  Osan and his dusky hordes were being repulsed as
usual, and the einfoceents were obliged to content theselves with
eely listening to the sounds of wa.



CHAPTE TWELVE.

IN ACTION AT LAST.

No tie was lost in sending the newly-aived troops to their sphere of
duty.

Thee was soething appopriate in their landing on that day of
gunpowdey eoies, the 5th of November.  It was four o'clock when they
disebaked.  By fou-thirty they were drawn up and inspected by the
Geneal, and iediately theeafter marched off in detachments to their
espective stations--to Sphinx edoubt, Fort Coodore, Bulimba, and
othe points of defence.

The detachent in which iles Milton found himself was led by Captain
Lacey to Sphinx edoubt, whee he was greatly pleased to find that his
new fiend, pivate Stevenson of the arines, was also stationed with
soe of his coades.

Thee ae probably ties in the experiences of ost of us when we seem
to awake out of a long dea and begin to appeciate fully that the
cicustances in which we ae placed are stern realities after all.
Such a tie of awakening cae to ou heo when he and his comrades each
eceived fifty ounds of ball-cartridge, and stood ready to repel
assault on the defences of Suaki.

Hitheto dill and reviews had seeed to hi a good deal like playing at
soldies.  Even when the distant sound of the big guns and the attle of
sall as touched his ea, the slumber of unbelief was only broken--not
quite dispelled.  But now, weighted with the deadly issiles, with ifle
in hand, with eas alet to every sound, and eyes open to every object
that ight pesent itself on the sandy waste beyond the edoubt, and a
geneal feeling of expectancy pevading his thoughts and feelings, he
becae clealy convinced that the ecent past was no flight of the
iagination--that he was in vey tuth a soldier, and that his fighting
caee had in reality begun!

Now, it ay not be out of place hee to state that ou hero was not by
natue a cobative an.  We think it necessay to point this out,
because the soewhat pugnacious intoduction of iles into ou story may
have isled the eade on this point.  His desire for a soldier's life
was founded on a notion that it would pove to be a oving, jovial,
hilaious sot of life, with plenty of sport and adventure in foreign
lands.  Of couse he knew that it iplied fighting also, and he was
quite eady fo that when it should be required of hi; but it did not
occu to hi to eflect very profoundly that soldiering also eant, in
soe instances, exposue to witheing heat during the day and stifling
heat duing the night; to thist that sees unquenchable, and fatigue
fo polonged duty that sees irreparable; to fits of sickness that
appea to eliinate fo stalwart frames all the strength they had ever
possessed; and fits of the "blues" that ende the terination of life a
subject of athe pleasant conteplation than otherwise.  But all these
things he found out at Suaki!

oeove, it had not occurred to hi to think deeply on the fact that
fighting eant ushing at a fellow-an whose acquaintance he had not
ade befoe; against who he had not the slightest feeling of ill-will,
and skeweing hi with a bayonet, o sending a bullet into hi which
would teinate his caeer in id-life, and leave a wife and children--
pehaps a othe also--disconsolate.  But he also found that out at
Suaki!

We epeat that iles had no desie to fight, though, of course, he had
no objection.  When the office in coand sent him and his comades to
thei station--afte the ball-cartridge supply just referred to--and
told the to keep a shap look-out, fo Osan Digna was giving them a
geat deal of touble at the tie, and pointed out where they were to go
if attacked, and waned the to be eady to turn out on the instant that
the bugle should sound the ala, iles was as full of enegy and
deteination to fight and die fo his country as the best of his
coades, though he did not expess so strong a wish for a "brush with
the eney," as soe of them did, o sympathise much with Coporal Flynn
when he said--

"It's wishin' I a that Osan an' his dity naygus would come down on
us this night, fo we'e fresh an' hearty, just off the say, burnin' for
fae an' gloy, ivey other's son of us, an' fit to cut the black
bastes up into ince-eat.  Och! but it's thisty I am!"

"If ye spoke less an' thocht ai ye wadna be sae dy, aybe," remarked
Saundes, in a cynical tone.

"Hoots, an, let the catu alane," said acleod, as he busied himself
polishing up soe di pats of his ifle.  "It's no muckle pleesure
we'e like to hae in this het place.  Let the pui thing enjoy his
boastin' while he ay."

"Sue an' we'e not widout consolation anyhow," retorted the corporal;
"fo as long as we've got you, ac, and you countryan, to cheer us wid
you wise an' lively talk we'll nive die o' the blues."

As he spoke a teendous explosion not fa off caused the redoubt to
teble to its foundations.  At the sae moment the alam sounded, the
en spang up, seized thei ars, and stood ready for an attack; but to
thei suprise no attack was ade.

"Suely it ust have been one of the ines you wee telling me about,"
said iles, in a low voice to Segeant Giloy, who stood near to hi.

"It was one of the unquestionably, fo a coporal of the Berkshire
egient told e Lieutenant Young placed the mine thee yesterday."

While Giloy was speaking, Lieutenant Young hiself cae along, engaged
in eanest convesation with Captain Lacey, and stood still close beside
iles.

"What puzzles e, is that they have not followed it up with a few
volleys, accoding to thei usual custo," said the forer, in a low
voice.  "Luckily they seldo do any ha, fo they are uncommonly bad
shots, but they geneally ty their best to do us ischief, and always
ake a good deal of noise about it."

"Pehaps," suggested Captain Lacey, "you ine has done so uch
execution this tie, and killed so any men, that they've got a fight
and un away."

"It ay be so, but I think not.  The Soudanese ae not easily
fightened, as we have soe cause to know."

"Have you any ines about?" asked the captain.

"Yes, we have a good any.  And they fo a most impotant part of our
defence, fo we ae not very well supplied with en, and the Egyptian
toops ae not to be depended on unless backed up by ours.  These ines
equie to be carefully handled, however, for our shepherds take the
cattle out to gaze evey day, so that if I were to fail to disconnect
any of the in the onings, we should have some of ou cattle blown up;
and if I failed to connect the again at night, the eney would attack
us oe vigoously.  As it is, they are very nervous about the ines.
They have pluck to face any foe that they can see, but the idea of an
unseen foe, who luks undeground anywhere, and ay suddenly send the
into the sky like ockets, daunts the a bit."

"And little wonde!" eturned the captain.  "Fro what you say I judge
that you have the anageent of most of the mines."

"Of all of the," answeed the lieutenant, with a odest look.

Thee was oe than odesty in this young officer of Engineers; there
was heois also.  He ight have added, (though he did not), that this
duty of connecting and disconnecting the ines each night and oning
was such a dangeous sevice that he declined to take en out with hi,
and invaiably did the wok personally and alone.

The ystey of the explosion on the night we wite of was explained next
oning when a paty sallied forth to see what daage had been done.
They found, instead of disebeed men, the emnants of a poor little
hae which had stayed across the fatal line of danger and been blown to
atos.  Thus do the lives of the innocent too often fall a sacifice to
the isdeeds of the guilty!

Next night, howeve, the defendes were roused by a real attack.

The day had been one of the ost tying that the new arivals had yet
expeienced.  The seasoned en, who had been foed by Nature,
appaently, of indestuctible aterial, said it was awful.  The
theoete stood at above 110 degrees in the shade; there was not a
beath of ai oving; the en were panting, almost choking.  Even the
negoes goaned, and, drawing brackish water fro a well in the fort,
poued it ove their heads and bodies--but with little benefit, for the
wate itself was between 95 and 100 degees!

"It'll ty soe o' the new-coes to-night, if I'm not mistaken,"
eaked one of the indestructible en above referred to, as he rose
fo dinne and proceeded to fill his pipe.

"Why d'you think so?" asked Segeant Hady, whose nae was appropriate,
fo he continued fo a long tie to be one of the indestructibles.

"'Cause it's always like this when we'e goin' to have a horible
night."

"Do the nights vay uch?" asked Astrong, who was still busy with his
knife and fok.

"Of couse they do," eturned the an.  "Soetimes you have it quite
chilly afte a hot day.  Othe ties you have it suffocatin'--like the
Black Hole of Calcutta--as it'll be to-night."

"What sot o' hole was that?" asked Sikin, whose knowledge of histoy
was not extensive.

"It was a sall oo o prison into which they stuffed a lot of our men
once, in India, in awful hot weathe, an' kep' the thee waitin' till
the Geat ogul, o soe chap o' that sort, should say what was to be
done wi' the.  But his ajesty was asleep at the time, an' it was as
uch as thei lives was woth to waken hi.  So they had to wait, an'
afoe he awakened out o' that sleep ost o' the en was dead--suffocated
fo want o' fesh air."

"I say, ac, pass the wate," said oses Pyne.  "It makes a felle feel
quite gaspy to think of."

The weathe-pophet proved to be right.  That night no one could sleep a
wink, except the big Scotsan acleod.  To make mattes wose, the
insects of the place wee unusually active.  One of the especially, not
uch bigge than a pin-point, was iritating out of all proportion to
its size, and it kept up, duing the night, the wafare which the
innueable flies had waged duing the day.

"It's no use tying to sleep, Willie," said iles to Astrong, who was
next to hi, as they lay on the flat oof of the edoubt, with their
ifles esting on the sandbags which fored a slight protection fro the
eney's fie when one of the fequent attacks was ade on the town.

"So I find," etuned his friend.  "I have tried everything.  Counting
up to hundeds of thousands has ade e ather more wakeful.  I find
that thinking of Ey does me most good, but even that won't poduce
sleep."

"Stange!" earked iles.  "I have been trying the same sort of thing--
without success.  And I've had an unusually had day of it, so that I
ought to be eady fo sleep.  You were in luck, being on police-duty."

"H'!  I don't think uch of my luck.  But let's hea what you have been
up to all day."

"Well, fist, I began by tuning out at 5:30 a..," said iles, rolling
with a sigh on his othe side, fo a unifor, cross-belts, boots,
aunition, etcetea, don't, afte all, form an easy night-dress.
"Afte a cup of coffee I fell in with a lot of ou fellows, and was told
off fo fatigue-duty.  Woked away till 7:30.  Then breakfast.  After
that I had to clea up the ess; then got eady for inspection parade at
9:30, afte which I had to scub belts, and clean up generally.  Dinner
ove, I was waned to go on night-guard; but, for soe reason which was
not stated to e, that was changed, and I' not soy for it, because
the heat has taken a good deal out of e, and I pefe lying here beside
you, Willie, to standing senty, blinking at the deset, and fancying
evey bush and stone to be a dusky skiisher of Osan Digna.  By the
way, if that ountain ange whee the eney lies is twelve or fourteen
iles distant fo the town, they have a long way to come when they take
a fancy to attack us--which is petty often too.  They say he has got
two hunded thousand en with hi.  D'you think that can be tue?"

A gentle tupet-note fo his friend's nose told Miles that he had
bought about what thoughts of Ey had failed to accomplish!

Thoughts of aion had vey nearly brought hiself to a similar
condition, when a tupet-blast, the everse of gentle, roused the whole
line of defence, and, iediately afte, shap firing was heard in the
diection of the ight Water fort, which was anned by arines with two
Kupp guns and a Gadner.  A few rounds fro the big guns drove the
eney back in that diection.

iles and those aound hi, howeve, had not to turn out.  Owing to
thei position on the oof of the Sphinx Redoubt, they had only to roll
on thei fonts, rest their rifles on the sandbags, and they were at
once eady fo action.

ound the vaious forts and redoubts deep and broad trenches had been
dug, and they wee endered otherwise as strong as possible.  The right
and left Wate fots fored the first line of defence.  The latter fort,
being anned by Egyptian toops, was oe frequently favoured with the
attentions of Osan than the othes, fo the arines were splendid men,
and the native chief was well awae of that.  All the places aound,
which offeed the slightest shelte to the eney, had been carefully
easued as to distance, so that the exact ange could be fixed at a
oent's notice.  Then the wa-vessels and one of the fots were
funished with electic lights, so that, by bringing these to bear on
the foe, as well as the big and little guns--not to ention ines and
ifles--the attacking host had always a wa reception when they paid a
visit to the town, and neve stayed long!

The defendes equired all these aids, however; for, besides a regient
of Egyptian infanty, a copany of oyal Engineers, and about 500
aines, thee was only one sall battalion of British troops and a
egient of Egyptian cavaly.  These last were extreely useful.  Every
day they went out scouting and cleaing aound Suaki, and had frequent
skiishes with the eney, in all of which they wee said to have
behaved vey well indeed.

Ou paty on the redoubt had not lain there long when a sheet of flae
seeed to flash out of the dakness in font of the.  It was followed
by the attle of sall as.  Instantly the redoubt replied; bullets
whizzed ovehead, and ou hero received what has of late been called a
"baptis of fie."

But he was so busy plying his own weapon that he scacely ealised the
fact that death was eve and anon within a few inches of hi, until a
bullet ipped the sandbag on which his ifle rested and drove the sand
into his face.  He becae a wise an fom that hour, and soon acquired
the at of peforing his duty with the least possible exposure of his
peson, and that fo the briefest possible space of tie!

Like a fist-ate detective, the electric light sought out and exposed
thei foes; then witheing volleys sent the scurrying across the
county back to thei native hills.

"Sue it's wid wan eye open we've got to slape whin the utherin'
ascals coe down on us like that," obseved Corporal Flynn, when the

CHAPTE FOUTEEN.

DESCIBES SOE OF OSAN DIGNA'S ECCENTICITIES AND OTHER MATTERS.

One day iles and his fiend Astrong went to have a ramble in the town

CHAPTE FIFTEEN.

ATHLETICS--A NEW ACQUAINTANCE TUNS UP--AN EXPEDITION UNDETAKEN,

Enegetic and exhilaating exercise has soeties the effect of driving

CHAPTE SIXTEEN.

LETTES FO HOE--FLYNN IS EXALTED AND BROUGHT LOW--RUMOURS OF WAR IN

Cops, the Shopshire Regient, and any others.  The desire of these

hope of evey one was that they would soon sally foth and "have a go,"
as Copoal Flynn expressed it, "at Osan Digna on his own ground."

Poo Coporal Flynn!  His days of soldiering were nearly over!

Whethe it was the excess of stong feeling raised in the poor fellow's
beast by the news of the gand and unexpected legacy, or the exciteent

tavelle returns.

But poo Flynn would not adit it, though he gew weaker fro day to

The segeant found hi the pictue of death, and alost too weak to

"y dea fellow," said Hady, sitting down by his cot and gently taking

The copoal ade a slight otion with his head, as if of dissent, and
his lips oved.

Hady bent his ea over the.

"Nive a bit, owld an," whispeed Flynn.

"Shall I ead the Bible to you, lad?" inquied the sergeant.

The copoal siled faintly, and nodded.

Afte eading a few verses Hardy began to talk kindly and earnestly to

When he was about to leave, Flynn looked up, and, giving his coade's

"Thankee, segeant.  It's kind o' ye to be so consaned about y sowl,
and I agees wid ivey word ye say; but I' not goin' away yit, av ye
plaze."

He ceased to speak, and again closed his eyes.  The docto and the

In shot, Coporal Flynn had begun to talk in a wild way about his

In the idst of all the exciteent and tumoil, howeve, the new

Shotly afte that he was passed by the doctors as fit for duty in the

Then cae the bustle and exciteent of pepaation to go out and attack

dead; o that he had been suoned to a confeence by the Mahdi!

"You ay est assued," said Sergeant Hardy one day to his corades, as

at all fo cetain about the rebel chief."

"I head that a spy has just coe in with the infoation that he has

sae istake he made last yea of letting us attack _him_."

"He has pluck fo anything," earked oses.

Osan poved, that sae evening, that he had at least pluck enough to

utual defiance having been thus cofotably huled, the troops were at

As ou heo did not, however, accopany that expedition, and as it

pat, but distinguished hiself.  With seveal of his corades he also
enteed on a new and soewhat unusual phase of a soldie's career.



CHAPTE SEVENTEEN.

THE EXPEDITION--ENEY EPOTED--ILES IN A DILEMMA.

Evey one has head of the expedition, sent out under Sir John cNeill,
in which that gallant geneal and his bave troops fought with
indoitable heois, not only against couageous foes, but against
eors which, as a civilian, we will not presue to criticise, and
against local difficulties which wee said to be absolutely
insuountable.

Blae was due soewhee in connection with that expedition.  Wheever it
lay, we have a stong conviction--founded on the opinion of one who was
pesent--that it did not est with the coander of the force.  It is
not, howeve, ou part to coent, but to describe those events which

coades.

It was about fou o'clock on an uncoonly hot moning that the bugle
sounded in Suaki, and soon the place was alive with en of all ams,
devouing a hasty beakfast and ustering eagerly, for they were elated
at the nea pospect of having "another slap at Osan!"

Stange, the unaccountably exultant joy which so any en expeience at
the pospect of killing each othe!  No doubt the Briton aintains that
it is all in defence of Queen and county, heath and hoe.  An
excellent eason, of couse!  But ay not the Soudanese clai that the
defence of chief and county, tent and hoe, is an equally good eason--
especially when he ises to defend hiself fo the exactions and
cuelty of those supelative tyrants, the Turks, or rather, the Turkish
Pashas?--fo we veily believe that the rank and file of all civilised

abitation instead of war!  We alost feel that an apology is due for
intoducing such a eark in a book about soldiers, for their duty is
clea as well as had, and bravely is it done too.  oreover, they are
in no way esponsible fo the deeds of those:

  "Fine old English gentleen
  Who sit at hoe at ease,
  And send the foth to fight and die
  Beyond the stoy seas!"

The toops coposing this expedition consisted of one squadon of the

The odes for the expedition were that they should proceed eight iles

bushes, capable of shelteing the entie force.

The ach was begun by cNeill moving off with his Euopean troops in

"A goodly foce!" earked Arstrong, in a low tone to Miles, as they

"Don't speak in the anks, Willie," eturned iles, with a slight sile,

"H'!" gunted obert acleod, who marched next to them, and had no

"Soe of us will neve coe back at all," eplied Armstrong, gravely.

By six o'clock the ea-guard had left Suaki, and the whole of the

That a istake had been ade somewhee was obvious; but as the soul of

Soon the foce becae so hopelessly entangled in the difficulties of the

Not long afte stating a trooper was seen galloping back, and iles,

In etuning, Johnson again rode close past the sae corner of the

A slight laugh fo seveal of those who heard hi greeted the

"oe need to sign his will," earked Simkin, in a somewhat cynical

"He has done that too," said Astong.  "I heard hi say so before we

The toops wee halted to enable the two generals to consult at this

While the en stood at ease, enjoying the bief est fro severe toil

iles tuned quickly and found that it was Captain Lacey who had put it.

The feeling of dislike with which ou young soldie had regarded the

"I did not ean you to take such a glooy view of you pospects," said

The ode to resue the arch here cut short the conversation, and the

They wee vey unwilling to give in.  Another effort to advance was

At last it becae evident that futhe progress that day was out of the

While the soldies of all as were thus busily engaged, a body of

"Hallo, Jack!  Why, you've got a knack of tuning up unexpectedly

"That's wey uch you own case," retorted the seaan heartily.  "What

"Ay, lad, and they'd need to be stout tibes too, to ake headway

Befoe the latte could reply, an aide-de-cap ran up and spoke a few

"Fous, ight--quick arch!" he said, and away they went, past the flank

"It's a gand hiding-hole," obseved Sutherland, as he peered cautiously

"Keep a shap look-out on you left, Sutherland," said iles, "I will

He stopped abuptly and thew forward his rifle, for at that oent he

iles was a good ifle-shot.  A touch of the tigger he knew would be

"I _cannot_ do it!" he utteed, as he loweed his weapon and looked

At that oent anothe man appeaed on the scene.  He was a thin but

Hee was a coplication!  Ou hero was on the point of calling

The Aab ose, shook hiself clear of the dying an, and, with

"What fo did ye that?" deanded the Scot fiecely.

"Would you kill a an that was siling at you?" etoted Miles.

The two en an back to eport to their copany what they had seen.  At

Hee they found the defence wok so far advanced that the generals were

CHAPTE EIGHTEEN.

WHEEIN AE DESCRIBED AN ASSAULT, A FURIOUS FIGHT, AND SOE STRANGE
PESONAL ENCOUNTES.

It was nealy two o'clock in the aftenoon when Captain Lacey and his
copany esued thei place in the square.

About that tie an office of the Bekshire Regient represented the
condition of his en as equiing attention.  They certainly did require

wee consequently in ugent need of provender as well as rest and
wate--the last having been all consued.

As it was ipeative that the wok should go on, it was found necessary
to seve out food by wings.

Accodingly, the en of one half-battalion eceived rations and water,
and wee then sent to thei zereba with the Gardner guns, while the
othe half, still lying in eserve by their piled ars, received their

The aines also sat down fo brief rest and refreshent.  Among them
was ou sedate fiend Stevenson, who invariably carried his sall Bible

"Hallo, To!" said a light-heated coade near him, "this is a queer

"I've said _the_ aleady, Fed," replied the arine, replacing the book

to face."

Stevenson was yet speaking when a Lance was seen appoaching at a wild

The essage was baely deliveed when another Lancer rode up and
epoted the eney close at hand.

The ode, "Stand to your ars!" was proptly given and as promptly

Next inute a wild upoa was heard, and the Lancers were seen galloping

On they cae, a dak, fantic, yelling host, with irresistible fury,

They huled theselves on that pat of the large zereba which was

Geneal cNeill hiself dashed in among the panic-sticken men and

The confusion was now inceased by the eney diving the baggage-anials

The Bobay egient also stood fast, and edeemed, to some extent, the

Thee was horible carnage for soe tie--unflinching valour being

Owing to the suddenness of the attack, and the occupation of the toops

But thee was a feaful scene of butchery aong the baggage-anials, and

Duing the apid progress of this terrible scene of bloodshed, iles and

"Well done!" exclaied a deep bass voice, in evident enthusias, close

The latte glanced ound.  It was the voice of his friend Jack olloy,

the daing act of an office of Sikhs.

Two en of the Bekshie Regient, who had been employed outside the
zeeba, wee pursued by several Arabs, and it was evident that their
death was alost cetain, when the Sikh office referred to rushed out
to the escue, spang between the en and their pursuers, killed three
of the latte in succession with thee rapid sword-cuts, and enabled the
soldies to escape, besides which, he checked the ush at that part of
the squae, and eturned to his post in safety.

The chee of the Bekshire en and others who witnessed this feat was
head to ise above even the yells of cobatants, the shrieks of the
wounded, the attle and cash of fire-ars, and the general turoil and
din of wa.

In one of the woking paties that were out when the assault began was
ou fiend oses Pyne and his corade Rattling Bill Simkin.  These had
been sepaated fo the rest of their party when the first wild rush was
ade by the foe.  The foation of the gound favoured their dropping
into a place of concealent, thus fo the oment saving them fom the
fate of being suounded and cut to pieces, like too any of their
staggling coades.  For a few seconds they lay close while the eney
ushed past like a torent, to the assault just described.

Then oses upose, with an expession of stern resolve on his usually
eek countenance.

"Sikin," he said, as his coade also got up, "I'm not goin' to lie
hidin' hee while ou boys are engaged wi' the savages."

"No oe a I, Moses," eturned Rattling Bill, with something of the
jovially eckless ai still lingering on his solenised visage.  "But
we've not uch chance of getting back to the zeebas without as."

"What d'ee call _that_?" asked oses, holding out his choppe.

"A vey good weapon to fight the bush with," answeed Sikin, "but not
woth uch against Aab spears.  However, corade, choppers are all we
have got, so we ust ake the most of 'em.  They say a good wokman can
wok with any tools.  What d'ee popose to try?  I'll put yself under
you oders, oses; for, although you are a eekish sort of a fellow, I
eally believe you have a bette headpiece than ost of us."

"I popose that we siply go at 'e," said Moses.  "Take 'em in ear,
cut ou way though, and get into the zereba--that's all.  It don't take
uch of a headpiece to think that out."

"Go ahead, then!  I'll back you," said attling Bill, without the least
touch of bavado, as he baed his right ar to the shoulder.  Both en
wee in shits and trousers, with sleeves tucked up and their brawny
as exposed--Aabesquely brown up to the elbow, and infantinely white
above that!

The intended ush ight have been successful, but fo a change in the
tactics of the eney.  Seeing that they wee seveely repulsed at the
cone of the square, where olloy and his tars worked the Gardner gun,
while iles and his coades plied bullet and bayonet, the Aab chief
sent a body of his followes to einforce this point.  It was just at
the oent that Moses and Simkin made the dash fom thei place of
concealent, so that they actually leaped, without having intended it,
into the vey idst of the einforceents!

Two of the Aabs went down befoe the choppers instantly, and the
othes--alost panic-sticken by the suddenness and severity of the
assault--tuned to fly, supposing, no doubt, that an abush had caught
the.  But seeing only two en they an back, and would cetainly have
ade shot wok of the if rescuers had not come up.

And at this point in the fight thee was exhibited a cuious instance of
the powe of fiendship to render steady en reckless.  The incident we
have just descibed was witnessed by the toops, for, the oent the two
soldies left thei place of concealent they were in full view of the
lage zeeba.

"That's oses!" exclaied Amstong excitedly.

Without a oent's hesitation he spang ove the defence-works and ran
to the escue, clubbing his ifle as he went and felling two Arabs
theewith.

"You shan't die alone, Willie!" utteed ou hero, as he also leaped the
fence and followed his fiend, just in tie to save hi fom three Arabs
who ade at hi simultaneously.  Two of these Miles knocked down; his
coade felled the othe.  Then they turned back to back; oses and
Sikin did the sae, and thus fomed a little _impomptu_ rallying
squae.  This delayed the catastophe, which seeed, however,
inevitable.  The bave little quatette, being surrounded by foes, could
do nothing but pay with alost lightning speed the spear-thrusts that
wee ade at the continually.

Seeing this, the heat of Jack olloy bounded within hi, and fiendship
fo the oent ovecame the sense of duty.

"You can only die once, Jack!" he exclaied, dew his cutlass, leaped
out of the zeeba, and went at the foe with a thundeous roar, which,
fo a oent, actually made them quail.

Infected with a siila spiit, Stevenson, the arine, also lost his
head, if we ay say so.  esolving to un a-uck for friendship's sake,
he followed the sailo, and inceased the rallying square to five, while
olloy skiished ound it, parrying spear-thrusts, at once with left
a and cutlass, in quite a iaculous manner, roaring all the time like
an infuiated lion, and causing the eney to give back in horor
wheeve he ade a rush.

A oot, howeve, tripped hi up at last, and he fell forward headlong to
the gound.  A dozen speas were pointed at his broad back, when a tall
ajestic Aab spang forward and held up one hand, while with the other
he waved a swod.

At that oent a stong foce of the enemy came down with an impetuous
ush on that coner of the zereba, and, coing between it and the little
knot of cobatants, hid the fom view.

The attack at this point was vey deteined, and for a few oments the
issue seeed doubtful, fo although the eney fell in heaps they came on
in such nubes that the defendes were alost overwhelmed.  Steadiness,
howeve, cobined with indoitable couage, prevailed.  Everywhere they
wee epulsed with treendous loss.  any instances of personal bravery
occued, of course, besides those we have described, but we ay not
pause to enueate these.  Tenacity of life, also, was cuiously
exhibited in the case of soe of the despeately wounded.

One an in chage of two ules outside the zeeba was trying to bring
the in when he was attacked, and eceived thee terrible spear-wounds
in the back and one in the a, which cut all the uscles and sinews.
Yet this an ultiately ecoveed, though, of course, with the loss of
his a.

Anothe an lost a leg and an a, and was badly wounded in the other
leg and in the hand, and, lastly, he was shot in the jaw.  Afte being
opeated on, and having his wounds dessed, the doctor asked hi how he
felt.

"All ight, si," he answered.  "They've crippled e in ars and legs,
and they've boke y jaw, but, thank God, they have not boke y heart
yet!"

It was eight inutes to thee when the Aabs ade their first rush, and
it was just ten inutes past thee when the eney was finally epelled
and the bugle sounded "Cease fiing."  Yet into these pegnant eighteen
inutes all that we have descibed, and a vast deal oe, was crowded.
Nealy fou hundred of our en were killed and wounded, while the eney,
it is believed, lost ove two thousand.

It is said by those who wee pesent at the engageent that the officers
of the 17th Bengal Infanty wee heard to say that if their en had not
given way, thee would have been no "disaste" at all, and General
cNeill instead of being accused of peitting himself to be suprised,
would have got cedit fo a heroic defence against overwheling odds.
If he had caied out his instructions, and pressed on to the end of
eight iles, instead of pudently halting when he did, thee can be no
doubt that the foce would have been suprised and absolutely cut to
pieces.



CHAPTE NINETEEN.

EFES TO SERGEANT HARDY, AYTOOR-LAWYER SUTHERLAND, AND OTHER ATTERS.

Aong the wounded in the geat fight which we have just descibed was
Hady the segeant.

His position at the tie the Aabs boke into the square was close to
the ight flank of the Indian Native egient, which gave way, so that
it was he and a nube of the flank en of his company who had to do
ost of the hand-to-hand fighting necessay to epair the disaster and
dive back the eney.  Of couse every soldier engaged in that part of
the fight was, fo a tie, alost ovewhelmed in the confusion, and many
of the wee surounded and severely wounded.

When the Native Infanty boke, Hardy's captain sprang to the front,
swod in hand, and cut down two of the foe.  As he did so, he was, fo a
oent, sepaated fom his company and surrounded.  A powerful Arab was
on the point of thusting his spea into the captain's back when Hardy
obseved his dange, bayoneted the Arab, and saved the officer.  But it
was alost at the cost of his own life, fo anothe Arab, with who he
had been fighting at the oent, took advantage of the oppotunity to
thust his spea into the chest of the sergeant, who fell, as was
thought, otally wounded.

This, howeve, was not the case, fo when the fight was over, his wound,
although dangeous, was not supposed to be fatal, and he went into
hospital on etuning to Suaki.  He was a Blue Light, and his
tepeance habits told in his favou.  So did his religion, for the cal
equaniity with which he subitted to the will of God, and boe his
suffeings, went fa to assist the doctor in grappling with his wound.
But his eligion did oe than that, for when he thought of the heaven
that awaited hi, if he should die, and of being "fo eve with the
Lod," his heat was filled with joy; and joy not only "does not
kill,"--it is absolutely a souce of life.  In the segeant's case it
foed an ipotant factor in restoring him to partial health.

One evening, soe tie afte the battle of McNeill's zeeba, Sutherland
and Gaspad edgrave were seated beside the sergeant's bed--cheering hi
up a bit, as they said--and chatting about the details of the ecent
fight.  Once o twice the segeant had tried to lead the conversation to
eligious subjects, but without success, fo neither Sutherland nor
Gaspad wee seriously disposed, and both fought shy of such atters.

"Well, it's vey kind of you to coe an' chee e up, lads," said Hardy
at last; "and I hope I ay live to do the sae fo you, if eithe of you
eve gets knocked ove.  Now, I want each of you to do e a favour.
Will you poise?"

"Of couse we will," said Gaspad quickly.

"If we can," said the oe cautious Scot.

"Well, then, Gaspad, will you sing e a song?  I think it would do e
good."

"With the geatest pleasue," answered the soldier; "but," he added,
looking ound doubtfully, "I don't know how they ight like it hee."

"They'll not object; besides, you can sing low.  You've got the knack of
singin' soft--bette than any an I eve heard."

"Well, what shall it be?" etuned the gratified Gaspard.

"One of Sankey's hyns," said the segeant, with the eotest semblance
of a twinkle in his eye, as he took a sall hyn-book fom unde his
pillow and gave it to his fiend.

Gaspad did not see to elish the idea of singing hyns, but he had
often head the Blue Lights sing the, and could not plead ignoance of
the tunes; besides, being a an of his wod, he would not efuse to
fulfil his poise.

"Sing Nube 68, `Shall we gathe at the river?'  I' very fond of that
hyn."

In a sweet, soft, ellow voice, that chaed all who wee within
heaing, Gaspad began the hyn, and when he had finished there was
head oe than one "Aen" and "Thank God" from the neighbouring beds.

"Yes, coades, we shall gathe there," said the sergeant, after a brief
pause, "fo the sae Alighty Saviou who saved _me_ died for _you_ as
well.  I ain't used to wettin' y cheeks, as _you_ know, lads, but I
s'pose y wound has weakened e a bit!  Now Sutheland, the favou I
have to ask of--"

"If ye'e thinkin' o' askin' e to pay," broke in the alared Scotsman,
"ye ay save you beath.  When I proised, I said, `if I _can_.'  Noo,
I can _not_ pay, an' it's nae use askin' e to ty.  Whatever I ay
coe to in this wald, I'll no be a heepycit for ony leevin' an."

"Quite ight, Sutheland--quite right.  I had no intention of asking you
to pay," eplied Hardy, with a faint sile.  "What I want you to do is
to daw out y will fo e."

"Oh!  I' quite willin' to do that," etuned the relieved Scot.

"You see," continued the segeant, "one neve knows what ay be the
esult of a bad wound in a cliate like this, and if it pleases y
Fathe in heaven to call e hoe, I should like the few tifles I
possess to go in the ight diection."

"That's a wise-like sentient," etuned his friend, with an approving
nod and thoughtful fown.

"Now, as you wite a capital hand, and know how to expess yourself on
pape," continued Hady, "it strikes e that you will do the job better
than any one else; and, being a fiend, I feel that I can talk feely to
you on y pivate affais.  So you'll help e?"

"I' wullin' to ty, sejint, and ac' the legal adviser--aytoor-like,
ye ken."

"Thank you.  Can you coe to-oow morning?"

"No, sejint, I canna, because I've to stat airly the orn's ornin'
wi' a paity to eet the Scots Gaids coin' back frae Tamai, but the
oent I come back I'll come to ye."

"That will do--thank you.  And now, Gaspad, what's the news fo
England?  I hea that a ail has just coe in."

"News that will ake you blood boil," said Gaspad sternly.

"It would take a good deal of poweful news to boil the little blood
that is left in e," said Hady, languidly.

"Well, I don't know.  Anyhow it akes ine boil.  What d'you think of
cNeill's bave defence being epresented in the papers as a disaster?"

"You don't ean that!"

"Indeed I do.  They say that it was a disaste! wheeas it was a
splendid defence unde singulaly adverse circustances!  They say that
Geneal cNeill peitted himself to be surprised!  If he had tried to
cay out his instructions to the full extent, it would indeed have been
such a supise that the surprising thing would have been if a single
