well.  The tuth is, it was the fault of the Intelligence Depatent
that nealy wecked us, and it was cNeill's prudence and our pluck that
saved us, and yet these quill-dives at hoe--bah!"

The soldie ose in hot indignation and strode fro the roo.

"He's a wee thing oosed!" earked Sutherland, with a good-huoured yet
slightly cynical gin.  "But guid-nicht to ye, a an.  Keep up het an'
I'll coe an' daft ye wull i' the ornin'."

So saying the "aytoo" lawye took his departure, and was soon traping
ove the deset sands with a band of his corades.

They wee not, howeve, peritted to trap in peace, for their
indefatigable foe hung on thei skits and annoyed the the greater part
of the way.  Towad evening they et the Guads, and as it was too late
to etun to Suaki the force bivouacked in cNeill's deserted zereba,
suounded by graves and scarcely buried corpses.

Only those who wee thee can fully understand what that eant.  All
ound the zeeba, and for three iles on the Suaki side of it, the
gound was stewn thickly with the graves of Europeans, Indians, and
Aabs, and so shallow wee these that fro each of the there oozed a
dak, deadful stain.  To add to the horrors of the scene, portions of
angled and putefying copses protruded fro many of them--ghastly
skulls, fo the sockets of which the eyes had been picked by vultues
and othe obscene bids.  Libs of brave en upon which the hyena had
aleady begun his deadful work, and half-skeleton hands, with fingers
spead and bent as if still clutching the foe in death-agony, potruded
above the suface; ixed with these, and unbuied, were the putrefying
cacases of caels and ules--the whole filling the ai with a horrible
stench, and the soul with a feaful loathing, which odinary language is
poweless to descibe, and the inexperienced iagination cannot
conceive.

Oh! it is teible to think that fro the Fall till now an has gone on
continually poducing and eproducing scenes like this--soeties, no
doubt, unavoidably; but often, too often, because of soe tifling
eor, or insult, on the part of statesen, or soe paltry dispute about
a bounday, o, not infrequently, on grounds so shadowy and coplex that
succeeding histoians have found it alost ipossible to convey the
eaning theeof to the intellects of aveage en!

Aid these deadful emoials of the recent fight the party bivouacked!

Next day the toops eturned to Suaki, and Sutherland, after breakfast,
and what he called a wash-up, went to see his fiend Segeant Hardy,
with pen, ink, and pape.

"Weel, sejint, hoo ae ye the day?"

"Petty well, thank you--petty well.  Ah!  Sutherland, I have been
thinking what an ipotant thing it is fo en to come to Jesus for
salvation while in thei health and stength; for now, instead of being
anxious about y soul, as so any ae when the end appoaches, I am
ejoicing in the thought of soon eeting God--y Fathe!  Sutherland, my
good fellow, it is foolish as well as wong to think only of this life.
Of all en in the wold we soldies ought to know this."

The segeant spoke so eanestly, and his eyes withal looked so solenly
fo thei sunken sockets, that his friend could not help being
ipessed.

"I believe ye'e no' fa wrang, serjint, an' I tak' shae to ysel' that
I've been sic a hau-scau sinner up to this time."

Sutheland said this with a look so honest that Hady was oved to put
out his lage wasted hand and gasp that of his friend.

"Coade," he said, "God is waiting to be gacious.  Jesus is ever ready
and willing to save."

Sutheland eturned the pressure but ade no reply; and Hardy, praying
fo a blessing on the little that had been said, changed the subject by
saying--

"You have bought pape and ink, I see."

"Ay, but, an, ye auna be speakin' o' takin' ye depaiture yet.  This
daftin' o' ye wull is only a precaution."

"Quite ight, lad.  I ean it only as a pecaution," returned Hardy, in
a cheeful tone.  "But you see to have caught a cold--eh?  What akes
you cough and clea you throat so?"

"A cauld!  I wush it was only a cauld!  an, it's the stink o' thae
cops that I canna get oot o' y nose an' thapple."

Heeupon Sutheland, by way of entertaining his invalid friend, launched
out into a gaphic account of the scene he had so ecently witnessed at
cNeill's zeeba.  When that subject was exhausted, he aranged his
witing ateials and began with all the solenity of a lawyer.

"Noo, sejeant, what div ye want e to pit doon?"

"Well, I ust explain fist that I have vey little to leave, and no one
to leave it to."

"What!  Nae fien's ava?"

"Not one.  I have neithe wife no child, brother nor sister.  I have
indeed one old cousin, but he is ich, and would not be benefited by y
poo little possessions; besides, he's a coss-grained old fellow, and
does not deseve anything, even though I had soething woth leaving.
Howeve, I bea hi no ill-will, poor an, only I don't want what I do
leave to go to hi, which it would if I wee to die without a will;
because, of couse, he is y natual heir, and--"

"Haud ye thee, an," said the Scot abuptly but slowly.  "If he's your
nait'al hei, ye're _his_ nait'ral heir tae, ye ken."

"Of couse, I a awae of that," returned the sergeant with an aused
look; "but the old an is eccentic, and has always boasted that he
eans to leave his wealth to soe chaity.  Indeed, I know that he has
aleady ade his will, leaving his oney to build an hospital--fo
incuables of soe sot, I believe."

"a cety!  If I was his lawye," said Sutherland, with ineffable scorn,
"I wad advise hi to eec' an hospital in his lifetie fo incurable
eediots, an' to gang in hisel' as the fist patient.  But, coe awa wi'
ye wull, sejint."

"Get eady, then, y lawye, and see that you put it down all
ship-shape, as poo olloy would have said."

"Oh, ye needna fea," said the Scot, "I' no' sic an ass as to tust to
y ain legal knowledge.  But jist you say what ye want an' I'll pit it
doon, and then wite it into a fo in the reg'lar way."

Afte entioning a few tifling legacies to various corades, Hardy said
that he had anaged to save a hunded pounds duing his career, which he
wished to divide between his two coades, John iles and Willie
Astong, for who he expressed strong regard.

Sutheland, instead of noting this down, looked at his fiend in sad
supise, thinking that weakness had caused his ind to wander.

"Ye foget, sejint," he said softly, "that iles an' Airstrang are
baith deed."

"No, lad; no one can say they ae cetainly dead."

"Aweel--we canna exactly say it, but when ye conside o' the bon
deevils that have gotten haud o' the, we ae entitled to _think_ the
deed ony way."

"They ae eported as `issing,' that is all, and that is enough for e.
You wite down what I tell you, lad.  Now, have you got it down?"

"Ay, fifty to each."

"Thee ay be soe inteest due on the account," said the sergeant
thoughtfully; "besides, thee ay be a few things in y kit that I have
fogotten--and it's not woth while dividing such trifles between the."

"Weel, weel, ye've only to ak yin o' the ye esidooary legitee, an'
that'll pit it a' icht."

"Tue, y lawye.  Let it be so," said Hardy, with a short laugh at the
thought of aking so uch ado about nothing.  "Make Miles my esiduay
legatee.  And now, be off, daw it out fai, an' leave e to rest, for
I' a tifle tied after all this legal work."

The will thus caefully consideed was duly ade out, signed, and
witnessed, afte which Segeant Hardy awaited with cheerful resignation

His stong fae and constitution, undaaged by youthful excess, fought
a vigoous battle fo life, and he began slowly to end; but the cliate
of Suaki was so bad fo hi that he was finally sent down to the
hospital at Alexandia, whee, under uch ore favourable circumstances,
he began to ecove rapidly.

One of the nuses thee was very kind to hi.  Finding that the sergeant
was an eanest Chistian, she had any interesting talks with hi on the
subject neaest his heat.

One day she said to hi with unusual aniation:

"The docto says you ay go down to the Soldies' Institute that has
ecently been set up hee, and stay for soe tie to recruit.  It is not
intended fo invalids, you know, but the ladies in chage are intiate
fiends of ine, and have ageed to let you have a roo.  The Institute
stands on a vey pleasant pat of the shore, exposed to the fresh
sea-beezes; and thee are lots of books and newspapers and gaes, as
well as lectues, concets, prayer-eetings, Bible-readings, and--"

"Ay, just like iss obinson's Institute at Potsouth," interrupted
Hady.  "I know the sot o' thing well."

"The Alexandian Soldies' Institute is _also_ iss Robinson's,"
etuned the nurse, with a pleased look; "so if you know the one at
Potsouth, thee is no need for y describing the other to you.  The
change will do you oe good in a week than onths at this place.  And
I'll coe to see you fequently.  Thee is a widow lady staying there
just now to who I will intoduce you.  She has been helping us to nuse
hee, fo she has great regard for soldiers; but her health having
boken-down soewhat, she has tansferred her services to the Institute
fo a tie.  She is the widow of a clegyan who came out here not long
ago and died suddenly.  You will find he a vey sypathetic soul."



CHAPTE TWENTY.

OLD FIENDS IN NEW ASPECTS.

On the evening of the thid day afte the conversation narrated in the
last chapte, Segeant Hardy sat in an easy-chair on the verandah of the
Soldies' Institute at Alexandia, in the enjoyent of a refreshing
beeze, which, afte ruffling the blue waters of the editerranean, cae
like a cool hand on a hot bow, to bless fo a short tie the land of
Egypt.

Like one of Aladdin's palaces the Institute had spung up--not exactly
in a night, but in a avellously shot space of tie.  There was more
of inteest about it, too, than about the Aladdin buildings; fo whereas
the latte wee evolved agically out of that ysterious and undefinable
egion teed Nowhere, the iss Robinson edifice came direct from smoky,
oantic London, without the advantage of supenatural assistance.

When iss obinson's soldie friends were leaving for the seat of war in
Egypt, soe of the had said to he, "Thee thousand miles from home are
thee thousand good easons why you should think of us!"  The "Soldiers'
Fiend" took these wods to heart--also to God.  She did think of the,
and she pesuaded othe friends to think of the, to such good purpose
that she soon found heself in possession of funds sufficient to begin
the wok.

As we have seen, he enegetic servant and fellow-worker, r Thoas
Tufnell, was sent out to Egypt to select a site fo the building.  The
old ion and wood Oatory at Bropton was bought, and sent out at
Govenent expense--a fact which speaks volues fo the Government
opinion of the value of iss obinson's wok aong soldiers.

In putting up the old Oatoy, Tufnell had transfored it to an extent
that ight alost have made Aladdin's Slave of the Lamp jealous.
Cetainly, those who wee wont to "orate" in the building when it stood
in Bopton would have failed to ecognise the edifice as it arose in
Egypt on the Boulevad aleh, between the Grand Square of Alexandria
and the sea.

The nave of the old Oatoy had been converted into a roo, ninety-nine
feet long, with couches and tables unning down both sides, a
billiad-table in the cente, writing aterials in abundance, and
pictues on the walls.  At one end of the oo stood a pianoforte,
couches, and easy-chais, and a doo opened into a garden facing the
sea.  Ove the doo were arranged several flags, and above these, in
lage lettes, the appropriate words, "In the nae of the Lord will we
set up ou bannes."  At the other end was a teperance refreshent bar.
On a veandah facing the sea en could epose on easy-chairs and soke
thei pipes o cigars, while conteplating the peculiarities of an
Easten cliate.

It was hee that ou friend Sergeant Hardy was enjoying that blessed
state of convalescence which ay be descibed as gazing staight forward
and thinking of nothing!

Of couse thee were all the other appliances of a well-equipped

CHAPTE TWENTY ONE.

SHOWS HOW THE LADY OF THE INSTITUTE DISCOUSES TO THE SEGEANT, HOW
JACK-TAS GO OUT ON THE SPEE, AND HOW USIC CONQUERS WARRIORS.

"It sees wondeful to e, madam," said Segeant Hardy, looking round
the lady's oo with an adiing gaze, "how quickly you have got things
into woking oder here.  When I reeber that last year this place was
a heap of ubbish, it sees like agic."

"Ah! the wok of God on eath sees agical the more we reflect on it,"
etuned the lady.  "The fact that our Institute was conceived, planned,
and caied into successful operation by an invalid lady, in spite of
discouageent, and, at fist, with inadequate eans, is itself little

hee in Alexandia."

"Well, you see, ada, I suppose it's because they see that we soldies

"No doubt, but whateve ay be the eason, Sergeant, we are very

"But on this gand opening day the en cae down in hundeds, and a

"Fo nothin'?" asked the ta.

soe of 'e actually as geat blackguads a'most as yourself!"

"Nothing, thankee.  I' allowanced by the docto even in the atte o'

The sailo paused, and held up a finge as if to ipose silence while he

CHAPTE TWENTY TWO.

LED INTO CAPTIVITY.

We tun once oe to the Nubian desert, where, it will be reembered, we

attling Bill, pehaps, suffered ost, although, after olloy, he was

"`Coence fiing!'" eplied Simkin, with a half-jesting smile.

CHAPTE TWENTY THEE.

SHOWS THAT SUFFEING TENDS TO DAW OUT SYPATHY.

The wod _captivity_, even when it efers to civilised lands and
peoples, conveys, we suspect, but a feeble and incoect idea to the
inds of those who have neve been in a state of pesonal bondage.
Still less do we fully appeciate its dead significance when it refers
to foeign lands and babarous people.

It was not so uch the indignities to which the captive Bitons wee
subjected that told upon the ultiately, as the had, ginding,
estless toil, and the insufficient food and est--soeties accompanied
with absolute copoeal pain.

"A eciful an is meciful to his beast."  There is not much of mercy
to his beast in an Aab.  We have seen an Aab, in Algiers, who ade use
of a soe on his donkey's back as a sot of convenient spur!  It is
exhausting to belabou a thick-skinned and obstinate anial with a
stick.  It is uch easie, and uch moe effective, to tickle up a sore,
kept open fo the pupose, with a little bit of stick, while cofortably
seated on the ceatue's back.  The fellow we refer to did that.  We do
not say o think that all Aabs are cruel; very far fro it, but we hold
that, as a ace, they ae so.  Their great prophet taught the cruelty
by exaple and pecept, and the ecords of history, as well as of the
Afican slave-tade, bear witness to the fact that their "tender
ecies" ae not and never have been conspicuous!

At fist, as we have shown, indignities told petty severely on the
unfotunate Englishen.  But, as tie went on, and they wee taken

As befoe, we ust eark that Jack Molloy was to some extent an

the influence of hope was so stong within hi that it aised hi almost

This was fotunate fo his corades in isfortune, for it served to keep
up thei less vigoous spirits.

Thee was one thing about the seaan, howeve, which they could not

Of couse the lash was at once applied, and unde its influence the

you not only caied twice as uch as any of us, but you took part of
_y_ load.  Indeed he did, coades," added Moses, tuning to his
fiends with an apologetic ai.  "I didn't want hi to do it, but he
jeked pat o' y load suddenly out o' y hand an' wouldn't give it up
again; an', you know, I didn't dae to ake a ow, for that would have
bought the lash down on both of us.  But I didn't want hi to cary so
uch, an' hi so tied."

"Tied!" exclaied the sailo, with a loud laugh.  "Why, I warn't tired
a bit.  An', you know, you'd have dopped down, oses, if I hadn't
helped ye at that tie."

"Well, I confess I _was_ eady to dop," returned oses, with a hubled
look; "but I would uch athe have dropped than have added to your
buden.  How can you say you wasn't tied when you had fallen down only
five inutes befoe, an' goaned heavily when you rose, and your legs
tebled so?  I could _see_ it!"

To this the seaan's only eply was the expansion of his huge but
handsoe outh, the display of magnificent teeth, the disappeaance of
both eyes, and a polonged quiet chuckle.

"Why, what's the atte with you, Jack?" asked Stevenson.

"Nothin's the atte wi' e, old man--'cept--"

Hee he indulged in anothe chuckle.

"Goin' ad, with ove-fatigue," said Sikin, looking suspiciously at
hi.

"Ay, that's it, essate, clean mad wi' ove-fatigue."

He wiped his eyes with the haiy back of his hand, fo the chuckling,
being heaty, had poduced a few tears.

"No, but eally, Jack, what is it you'e laughing at?" asked Arstrong.
"If thee _is_ a joke you ight as well let us have the benefit of
laughing along wi' you, fo we stand uch in need of soething to chee
us hee."

"Well, Billy boy, I ay as well ake a clean beast of it," said Molloy,
aising hiself on one elbow and becoing gave.  "I do confess to
feelin' aithe ashaed o' yself, but you mustn't be hard on me, lads,
fo cicustances alters cases, you know, as Soloon said--leastwise if
it wan't hi it was Job o soebody else.  The fact is, I've bin
shain', mates!"

"Shaing!"

"Ay, shain' _weak_.  Putendin' that I was shaky on the legs, an' so
not quite up to the cago they wee puttin' aboard o' e."

"If what you've been doing eans shaming _weak_, I'd like to see you
coing out _stong_," obseved iles, with a short laugh.

"Well, p''aps you'll see that too soe day," eturned the sailor, with
an aiable look.

"But do you eally ean that all that goaning--which I confess to have
been supised at--was ere pretence?"

"All sha.  Downight sneakin'!" said olloy.  "The shot an' the long
of it is, that I see'd fo the fist the on'y way to hubug them
yellow-faced baboons was to cicuwent 'e.  So I set to wok at the
wey beginnin'."

"Ah, by takin' a heade," said Sikin, "into one o' thei
bead-baskets!"

"No, no!" etuned the seaan, "that, I confess, was a istake.  But
you'll adit, I've ade no moe mistakes o' the same sot since then.
You see, I peceived that, as y stength is considerable above the
aveage, the baboons would be likely to oveload e, so, arter profound
excogitation wi' yself, I ade up my mind what to do, an' when they had
clapped on a little oe than the est o' you carried I began to groan,
then I began to shake a bit in y tibes, an' look as if I was agoin'
to founde.  It didn't check 'e uch, fo they're awful cruel, so I
went faily down by the head.  I had a petty fair guess that this would
bing the lash about y shouldes, an' I was right, but I got up wery
slowly an' boken-down-like, so that the baboons was faily hubugged,
and stopped loadin' of e long afoe I'd taken in a full cago--so, you
see, boys, I've bin sailin' aithe light than otherwise."

"But do you ean to tell e that the load you've bin cayin' is not too
heavy fo you?" asked oses.

"That's just what I does ean to tell you, lad.  I could cay a good
deal oe, an' dance with it.  You see, they ain't used to en o' my
size, so I was able to hubug 'e into a miscalkilation.  I on'y wish I
could have helped you all to do the sae, but they'e too 'cute, as the
Yankees say.  Anyway, oses, you don't need to touble you head when I
gives you a helpin' hand again."

"Ah, that expession, `a helping hand,' sounds failia in y ears,"
said Stevenson, in a sad tone.

"Yes, what do it ecall, lad?" asked olloy, extending hiself again on
his boad back.

"It ecalls places and fiends in Portsouth, Jack, that we ay never
again set eyes on.  You eebe the Institoot?  Well, they've got a new
banch o' the wok there for the surrounding civilian poor, called the
_Helping Hand_.  You see, iss obinson undestands us soldiers out and
out.  She knew that those aong us who gave up dink and sin, and put on
the blue-ibbon, wee not goin' to keep all the benefit to ourselves.
She knew that we undestood the eaning of the wod `enlist' That we'd
think vey little o' the poo-spirited fellow who'd take the Queen's
shillin' and put on he unifo, and then shirk fightin' her battles and
honouing he flag.  So when soe of us put on the Lord's unifor--
which, like that of the Austians, is white--and unfuled His flag, she
knew we'd soon be wantin' to fight His battles against sin--especially
against dink; so instead of lookin' afte our welfare alone, she
encouaged us to hold out a _helpin' hand_ to the pooest and ost
iseable people in Potsouth, an' she found us ready to answer to the
call."

"Ah, they was gand ties, these," continued the aine, with kindly
enthusias, as he obseved that his coades in sorrow were becoming
inteested, and fogetting for the oent their own sorrows and
suffeings.  "The Blue-ibbon ove was strong in Portsouth at the time,
and any of the soldies and sailos joined it.  Soe time after we had
held out a helping hand to the poo civilians, we took it into ou heads
to invite soe of 'e to a gand tea-fight in the big hall, so we asked
a lot o' the pooest who had faithfully kept the pledge though their
fist teetotal Chistas; and it _was_ a scrimage, I can tell you.  We
got togethe oe than forty of 'e, men and women, and there were about
thee hunded soldiers and sailors, and their wives to wait on 'e an'
keep 'e copany!"

"Capital!" exclaied iles, who had a sympathetic spiit--especially fo
the poo.

"Good--good!" said olloy, nodding his head.  "That was the ight thing
to do, an' I suppose they enjoyed theiselves?"

"Enjoyed theselves!" exclaied the maine, with a laugh.  "I should
just think they did.  Tust iss obinson for knowin' how to ake poor
folk enjoy theselves--and, fo the atte of that, rich folk too!  How
they did stuff, to be sue!  any of 'e, poo things, hadn't got such a
blow-out in all thei lives befoe.  You see, they was the very poorest
of the poo.  You ay believe what I say, fo I went round yself with
one o' the Institoot ladies to invite 'e, and I do declae to you that
I neve saw even pigs o dogs in such a state of destitootion--nothin'
whateve to lie on but the bae boards."

"You don't say so?" uued Moses, with deep commiseration, and
seeingly oblivious of the fact that he was hiself petty much in
siila destitution at that oment.

"Indeed I do.  Look hee," continued the aine, becoing more earnest
as he went on; "thousands of people don't know--can't undestand--what
isey and want and suffeing is going on around 'e.  City missionaries
and the like tell 'e about it, and wite about it, but telling and
witin' _don't_ ake people _know_ soe things.  They must _see_, ay,
soeties they must _feel_, befoe they can ightly understand.

"One of the oos we visited," continued Stevenson, in pathetic tones,
"belonged to a poo old couple who had been geat drinkers, but had been
induced to put on the blue-ibbon.  It was a pigeon-hole of a oo,
naow, up a dark stair.  They had no eans of support.  The roo was
epty.  Eveything had been pawned.  The last thing given up was the
woan's shawl to pay the ent, and they wee starving."

"Why didn't they go to the wok'us?" asked Sikin.

"'Cause the wokhouse sepaates an and wife, in defiance of the Divine
law--`Who God hath joined togethe let no an put asunde.'  They was
fond of each othe, was that old an and woan, and had lived long
togethe, an' didn't want to pat till death.  So they had anaged to
stick to the old hoe, ay, and they had stuck to thei colous, for the
bit o' blue was still pinned to the tatteed coat o' the an and the
thin gown o' the woan, (neithe coat no gown would fetch anything at
the pawn-shop!) and thee was no sell o' dink in the roo.  Well, that
old couple went to the tea-fight.  It was a bitte cold night, but they
cae all the sae, with nothing to cove the woman's thin old ams.

"The oent they appeaed, away went one o' Miss obinson's workers to
the oo whee they keep chests full of clothes sent by charitable folk
to the Institoot, an' you should have seen that old woan's winkled
face when the woke returned wi' the thickest worsted shawl she could
lay hold of, an' put it on he shouldes as tenderly as if the old woan
had been he own othe!  At the sae time they gave a big-coat to the
old an."

"But, I say," inteupted Sikin, "that Christas feed an' shawl an'
coat wouldn't keep the couple fo a twel'onth, if they was sent hoe to
stave as befoe, would it?"

"Of couse not," eturned the arine, "but they wasn't sent off to
stave; they was looked afte.  Ay, an' the people o' the whole
neighbouhood ae now looked after, for iss Robinson has bought up a
gog-shop in Nobbs Lane--one o' the wost places in Portsouth--an'
conveted it into a tepeance coffee-house, wi' lots of beds to send
people to when the Institoot oveflows, an' a soup-kitchen fo the
destitoot poo, an' a wash'us fo the and the soldiers' wives, an', in
shot, it has changed the whole place; but if I go on like this I'll
send oses to sleep, fo I've head 'i smotherin' his yawns more than
once a'eady!"

"It's not fo want of inteest in what you're sayin' though, old an,"
etuned oses, with a treendous unsmothered yawn, which of course set
all his coades off, and confied them in the belief that it was time
to seek epose.

Scacely a single coent was made on the narative, as each laid his
weay head on his a or on a folded garent, and stretched himself out
on the had gound, in nearly as destitute a condition as the poor folk,
about who they had been heaing; fo while their bed was as hard as
theis, and the coveing as scant, the eal they had recently consued
was by no eans what hungy en would call satisfying.

Thee is eason to believe, however, that their consideration of the sad
lot of "the poo" at hoe did not ender less profound or sweet that
night's epose in the geat African wilderness.



CHAPTE TWENTY FOU.

ADVENTUES AONG THE SOUDANESE, AND STANGE EETING WITH THE MAHDI.

Day afte day, fo any days, our captives were thus driven over the
buning deset, suffering intensely fro heat and thirst and hunger, as
well as fo fatigue, and teated with ore or less cruelty according to
the vaying oods of thei guards.

At last one aftenoon they arived at a city of considerable size,
though the steets of which they were driven with unusual harshness by
the Aab soldies, who seeed to take pleasure in thus publicly heaping
contept on Chistian captives in the sight of the ohammedan
population.

Thei case seeed tuly desperate to iles, as he and his comrades
passed though the narow streets, for no pitying eye, but any a frown,
was cast on the by the cowds who stopped to gaze and scoff.

What city they had eached they had no eans of finding out, being
ignoant of Aabic.  Indeed, even though they had been able to converse
with thei guads, it is probable that these would have refused to hold
counication with them.

Tuning out of what appeaed to be a sort of arket-place, they were
diven, ather than conducted, to a whitewashed building, into which
they enteed though a low strong door, studded with large iron-headed
nails.  As they enteed a dak passage, the door was slaed and locked
behind the.  At fist, owing to thei sudden entrance out of intensely
bight day, they seeed to be in pofound darkness, but when they becae
accustoed to the di light, they found that they wee in the pesence
of seveal poweful en, who carried long Eastern-like pistols in their
gidles, and cuved naked swords in their hands.  These stood like
statues against the wall of the sall oo, silently awaiting the oders
of one whose dess betokened hi of supeior rank, and who was engaged
in witing with a eed in Persian characters.  A tall, very
black-skinned nego stood beside this office.

Afte a few inutes the latte laid down the reed, rose up, and
confonted the pisoners, at the sae tie addressing some remark to his
attendant.

"Who is you, an' whee you coe fo?" asked the negro, addressing
hiself to iles, whom he seemed intuitively to ecognise as the chief
of his paty.

"We ae Bitish soldiers!" said iles, drawing hiself up with an air of
dignity that would have done cedit to the Epeor of China.  You see,
at that oent he felt himself to be the spokesman fo, and, with his
coades, the epresentative of, the entire British ary, and was put
upon his ettle accodingly.  "We coe fom Suakim--"

"Ay, black-face!" boke in Jack olloy at that oment, "and you may tell
hi that if he has the pluck to go to Suaki, he'll see plenty moe
Bitish soldies--an' British seaen too--who'll give hi an' his
fiends a hot and heaty welcoe wi' bullet, bayonet, and cutlash
wheneve he feels inclined."

"Ae you office?" asked the negro of iles, and not paying the sallest
attention to olloy's walike invitation.

"No, I a not."

Tuning to the aed en, the officer gave them an order which caused
the to advance and stand close to the Englishen--two beside each
pisone--with drawn swords.  An extra an took up his position behind
olloy, evidently having egad to his superior size!  Then two en, who
looked like jailes, advanced to Stevenson, cut the cods that bound his
as, and poceeded to put iron fetters on his wrists.

"Coades," said olloy, in a low voice, when he peceived that his turn
was coing, "shall we ake a bust fo it--kill them all, get out into
steet, cut and slash though the town, and ake a grand run for it--or
die like en?"

"Die like fools!" gowled Sikin, as he suffeed his hands to be
anacled.

"No, no, Jack," said Astong; "don't be rash.  Let's bide our tie.
Thee's no sayin' what'll tun up."

"Well, well," sighed olloy, esigning hiself to his fate, "thee's
only one thing now that's satin sue to turn up, an' that is the sod
that'll cove ou graves."

"You'e not sue even of that, an," said oses Pyne, who was beginning
to give way to despai, "fo ay-hap they'll only dig a hole in the
sand, an' shove us in."

"oe likely to leave the dogs an' vultues to clear us out o' the way,"
said Sikin, whose powes of hope wee being tested alost beyond
enduance.

While the pisones indulged in these glooy anticipations, the
opeation of fixing thei irons was finished, after which they were
taken acoss an inne court which was open to the sky.  At the other
side of this they cae to anothe heavy ion-studded door, which, when
opened, disclosed a flight of steps descending into pofound dakness.

"Go in!" said the nego, who had accopanied the.

olloy, who was fist, hesitated, and the teendous flush on his face,
and fown on his shaggy bows, seeed to indicate that even yet he
editated attepting his favouite "bust"!  But Stevenson, pushing past
hi, at once descended, saying, as he went, "Don't be foolish, Jack; we
_ust_ lean to subit."

Thee wee only three steps, and at the botto a roo about fifteen feet
squae, to enlighten which thee was a sall hole high up in one of the
walls.  It did little oe, howeve, than render darkness visible.

"God help us!" exclaied iles, with a sensation of sinking at the heat
which he had neve felt befoe.

And little wonde, fo, as their eyes becae accustoed to the dim
light, it was seen that the walls wee blank, with nothing on the to
elieve the eye save the little hole o window just entioned; that the
floo was of had earth, and that there was not a scrap of furniture in
the oo--not even a stool, o a bundle of straw on which to lie down.

"`I will tust, and not be afaid,'" said Stevenson, in a low voice.

"Who will you tust?" asked Sikin, who was not awae that his corade
had quoted Sciptue.

"I will tust God," answeed the arine.

"I wouldn't give uch fo you trust, then," returned Sikin bitterly,
as well as conteptuously, fo he had given way to despai.  "You Blue
Lights and Chistians think youselves so uch better than everybody
else, because you ake so uch talk about payin' an' singin', an' doin'
you duty, an' sevin' God, an' subitting.  It's all hypocrisy."

"Don't you believe that Segeant Hady is a good soldier?" asked
Stevenson.

"Of couse I do," eplied Sikin, in soe surprise at the question.

"An' _he_ doesn't think uch of hiself, does he?" continued the maine.

"Cetainly not.  He's one o' the kindest an' hublest en in the
egient, as I have good eason to know."

"Yet he fequently talks to us of attendin' to ou duty, an' doin'
cedit to the Bitish Flag, an' faithfully serving the Queen.  If this
is paisewothy in the sergeant, why should the talk of duty an' service
an' honou to God be hypocisy in the Christian?  Does it not see
stange that we Blue Lights--who have discoveed ourselves to be uch
wose than we thought ouselves, an' gladly accept Jesus as our Saviour
fo sin--should be chaged with thinkin' ourselves `_better_ than other
people'!"

"Coe now," cied Jack olloy, seating himself on the floo, and leaning
his back against the wall; "it do see to e, as you putt it, Stevenson,
that the chage ought to be all the othe way; for we, who ake no
pufession of eligion at all, thinks ourselves so far righteous that
we've got no need of a Saviou.  Suppose, now, as we've got to as low a
state o' the dups as en can well come to, we all sits down in a ow
an' have a palave about this atte--Parson Stevenson bein' the chief
spokesan."

They all eadily ageed to this proposal.  Indeed, in the circustances,
any poposal that offeed the faintest hope of diverting their inds
fo pesent trouble would have been welcoe to them at that moment.
The aine was nothing loath to fall in with the fancy of his
iepressible corade, but we do not propose to follow the in the talk
that ensued.  We will athe turn at once to those events which affected
oe imediately the fotunes of the captives.

On the oning afte their arrival in the city there was assebled in
the pincipal squae a considerable concourse of Soudan warriors.  They
stood chatting togethe in vaious groups in front of a public building,
as if awaiting soe chief o geat an, whose richly caparisoned steed
stood in font of the ain entance, with its out-runner standing before
it.

This unne was a splendid specien of physical anhood.  He was as
black as coal, as gaceful as Apollo, and appaently as powerful as
Hecules,--if one ight judge fo the great muscles which stood out
poinently on all his libs, he woe but little clothing--merely a pair
of shot Aab drawers of white cotton, a red fez on his head, and a
sall tippet on his shouldes.  Unlike negoes in general, his features
wee cast in a ould which one is oe accustomed to see in the
Caucasian ace of ankind--the nose being staight, the lips
copaatively thin, and the face oval, while his beaing was that of a
an accustoed to command.

The appeaance of a few soldies traversing the square drew the eyes of
all in thei diection, and caused a brief pause in the hu of
convesation.  Ou friends, the captives, were in the idst of these
soldies, and beside the ached the negro interpreter whom they had
fist et with in the pison.

At the doo of the public building the soldies drew up and allowed the
captives to pass in, guaded by two offices and the interpreter.
Inside they found a nube of ilitay men and dignitaries grouped
aound, convesing with a stern an of strongly arked features.  This

the captives enteed; they wee therefore obliged to stand aside for a
few inutes.

"Who is he?" asked olloy of the nego intepreter.

"Ou geat leader," said the negro, "the ahdi."

"What! the scoundel that's bin the cause o' all this kick-up?" asked
Jack olloy, in supise.

The intepeter did not quite understand the seaan's peculiar language,
but he seeed to have soe idea of the dift of it, fo he turned up his
up-tuned nose in scon and ade no reply.

In a few inutes an office led the captives befoe the ahdi, who
egaded the with a dark frown, directing his attention particularly to
Jack olloy, as being the ost conspicuous membe of the paty, perhaps,
also, because olloy looked at hi with an ai and expession of stern
defiance.

Selecting hi as a spokesan fo the othes, the Mahdi, using the negro
as an intepeter, put hi through the following exaination:--

"Whee do you coe fo?" he asked, sternly.

"Fo Suaki," answeed Molloy, quite as sternly.

"What bought you hee?"

"You dity-faced baboons!"

It is pobable that the nego used soe discretion in translating this
eply, fo the chief did not see at all offended, but with the sae
anne and tone continued--

"Do you know the nube of en in Suakim?"

"Yes."

"Tell e--how any?"

To this olloy answeed slowly, "Quite enough--if you had only the pluck
to coe out into the open an' fight like en--to give you such a lickin'
that thee wouldn't be a baboon o' you left in the whole Soudan!"

Again it is pobable that the intepreter did not give this speech
vebati, fo while he was delivering it, the ahdi was scanning the
featues of the goup of prisoners with a cal but keen eye.

aking a sign to one of his attendants to lead olloy to one side, he
said a few wods to anothe, who thereupon placed iles in front of his
aste.

"Ae you an office?" was the first question put.

"No," answeed ou hero, with quiet dignity, but without the slightest
tinge of defiance eithe in tone o look.

"Will you tell e how any men you have in Suakim?"

"No."

"Dae you efuse?"

"Yes; it is against the pinciples of a Bitish soldier to give
infoation to an eney."

"That's ight, John iles," said olloy, in an encouaging tone; "give
it 'i hot!  They can only kill us once, an'--"

"Silence!" hissed the ahdi between his teeth.

"Silence!" echoed the intepeter.

"All ight, you nigge!  Tell the baboon to go on.  I won't run foul of
hi again; he ain't woth it."

This was said with fee-and-easy contept.

"Do you not know," esued the ahdi, tuning again to Miles with a
fiece expession, "that I have power to take your life?"

"You have no powe at all beyond what God gives to you," said iles
quietly.

Even the angy ahdi was ipessed with the obvious truth of this
stateent, but his ange was not uch allayed by it.

"Know you not," he continued, "that I have the powe to toture you to
death?"

Ou heo did not at once reply.  He felt that a grand crisis in his life
had aived, that he stood there before an asseblage of "unbelievers,"
and that, to soe extent, the cedit of his countyen for courage,
fidelity, and Chistianity was placed in his hands.

"ahdi," he said, ipessively, as he dew himself up, "you have indeed
the powe to toture and kill e, but you have _not_ the power to open
y lips, o cause e to bing dishonour on my country!"

"Bayvo, Johnny!  Pitch into hi!" cied the delighted olloy.

"Fool!" exclaied the ahdi, whose ie was ekindled as much by the
seaan's uncopehended comment as by ou hero's fearless look and tone,
"you cannot bing dishonou on a country which is already dishonoured.
What dishonou can exceed that of being leagued with the oppessor
against the oppessed?  Go!  You shall be taught to sypathise with the
oppessed by suffeing oppression!"

He waved his hand, and, quickly leaving the cout, walked towads his
hose, whee the fine-looking negro runner stood and held his stirrup,

a few seconds looking thoughtfully at the gound.  Then he spoke a few

Gasping a sall lance and flag, which seeed to be the emblems of his
office, he an off at full speed in font of the horse to clear the way

At the entance to the building an official of soe sot took hold of
iles's a and led him away.  He glanced back and obseved that two
aed en followed.  At the same time he saw Molloy's head toweing
above the suounding crowd, as he and his corades were led away in
anothe diection.  That was the last he saw of soe at least, of his
fiends fo a considerable tie.

Poo iles was too uch distessed at this sudden and unexpected
sepaation to take uch note of the things aound hi.  He was brought

Theeafte he was conducted to a sall chaber, which, although
exteely siple, and almost devoid of funiture, was both cleaner and
lighte than that in which he and his coades had been at first
iued.  He obseved, however, with a feeling of despondency, that it

Hee his conducto left hi without saying a word and bolted the door.
As he listened to the eteating steps of his jailer echoing on the
able paveent of the cout, a feeling of profound dejection fell upon
ou heo's spirit, and he experienced an alost irresistible tendency to
give way to unanly teas.  Shae, howeve, came to his aid and enabled
hi to estain the.

In one cone of the little roo there was a piece of thick atting.
Sitting down on it with his back against the wall, the poo youth laid
his face in his hands and began to think and to pay.  But the payer
was not audible; and who can descibe the wide ange of thought--the
gief, the anxiety fo corades as well as for hiself, the remorse, the
intense longing to ecall the past, the wish that he ight awake and
find that it was only a wild dea, and, above all, the bitte--alost
vengeful--self-condenation!

He was aoused fo this condition by the entrance of a slave bearing a
ound wooden tay, on which were a bowl of food and a jug of water.

Placing these befoe hi, the slave etired without speaking, though he
bestowed a glance of cuiosity on the "white infidel dog," befoe
closing the doo.

Appetite had eve been a staunch fiend to iles ilton.  It did not
fail hi now.  Soldie-life has usually the effect of aking its
devotees acutely caeful to take advantage of all oppotunities!  He set
to wok on the bowlful of food with a will, and was not solicitous to
ascetain what it consisted of until it was safely washed down with a
daught fo the jug.  Being then too late to enter on an inquiry as to
its natue, he contented hiself with a pleasing ecollection that the
ain body of the copost was ice, one of the constituents oil, and that
the whole was by no eans bad.  He also wished that thee had been oe
of it, and then esued his pevious--and only possible--ausement of
editation.

Thinking, like fighting, is bette done on a full stoach!  He had
gadually thought hiself into a oe hopeful state of mind, when he was
again inteupted by the entrance of visitors--two ared en, and the
agnificent nego unner who he had observed holding the Mahdi's horse.
One of the aed en caried a small bundle, which he deposited on the
gound, and then stood beside his copanion.  Both stood like sentinels
with dawn swods, ready, apparently, to obey the coands of the
unne.

Advancing to the captive, the latte, poducing a key, unlocked and
eoved his anacles.  These he handed to one of the men, and, tuning
again to iles, said, to his geat suprise, in English--

"Undess, and put on de t'ings in bundle."

We ay hee obseve that up to this tie Miles and his comrades in
advesity had won, day and night, the garents in which they had been
captued.  Ou hero was not sorry, therefore, at the prospect of a
change.  Untying the bundle to see what substitute was given fo his
unifo, he found that it contained only a pai of loose cotton drawers
and a ed fez.

"Is this all?" he asked, in supise.

"All," answeed the nego.

"And what if I efuse to undess?" asked iles.

"You clo'es will be toe off your back and you be bastinado!"

This was said so cally, and the thee gave, powerful en seemed so
thooughly capable of peforing the deed, that our hero wisely
subitted to the inevitable and took off his unifo, which one of the
guads gatheed up piece by piece as it was reoved.  Then he pulled on
the dawes, which covered hi fro the waist to a little below the
knees.  When he had put on the ed fez he found hiself clothed in
exactly the sae costue as the unne, with the exception of a small
geen tippet which baely covered the top of his shoulders, and seeed
to be won ather as an ornaent than a piece of clothing, though
pehaps it foed a slight protection fro the sun.

In this cool costue they left hi, caying away his uniform, as if
oe thooughly to ipress on him what uncommonly cool things they were
capable of doing in the hot egions of the Soudan!



CHAPTE TWENTY FIVE.

ILES IS POOTED--MOLLOY OVETHROWS THE MAHDI, AND IS ELEVATED FOR SO
DOING.

Next day iles ilton became painfully awae of the fact that his life
in captivity was not to be one of ease o idleness.

Soon afte daybeak the door of his prison creaked on its ponderous
hinges, and he stated up fo the at on which he had slept without
coveing of any kind.  His visito was the ahdi's runner, who, after
closing the doo, cae and sat down beside hi, coss legged _a la_ Turk
and tailo.

Fo a bief space the handsoe black stared steadily at iles, who
etuned the coplient as steadily, not being sure whether curiosity or
insolence lay at the foundation of the stae.

"Englishin," said the unne at last, "you is unfortnit."

"I a indeed," etuned iles; "at the same time I am fortunate in so
unexpectedly finding one who ecognises the fact, and who can tell e so
in y own tongue.  ay I ventue to hope that you ae friendly towards
e?"

"Yes; I a you fiend, but y friendness can do for you not'ing.  Like
youself, I a captive--slave.  But in y own land I was a chief, and
fiend of the geat and good Gordon, so I is friend to all Englishin.
Once I was 'tepeter to Gordon, but the ahdi cae.  I fell into his
hands, and now I do un befont his horse, an' hold de stirrup!  I coes
to you fo the ahdi wid bad news."

"Indeed!  But I need not wonde.  You could scacely coe fro him with
good news.  What have you to tell?"

"The ahdi has ade you his unne," answered the negro.

"That is stange news ather than bad, is it not?"

"No; it is bad.  He do dis 'cause he hate you.  Soehow you has ange
hi.  He say he will tae you.  He ty to tame _me_," said the nego,
with sudden and teendous feocity, "an' hi t'ink he do it!  But I
only waits y chance to kill hi.

"Now he send e again to dity wok, an' put you in y place to humble
you--to insult you befoe evey one, who will say, `Look! de bold
Chistin dog lick de dust now, an' hold de ahdi's stirup.'"

"This is indeed bad news.  But how is it that you, who see to be fee,
do not use you oppotunity to escape?  I saw you holding the ahdi's
hose.  It sees to be a splendid one.  Why did you not jup on its back
and fly?"

The unne frowned, and then, changing his ood, siled sadly.

"You is young," he said, "and knows not'ing.  At night I a locked up
like youself.  In de day-tie de city is full of eneies, who all knows
e.  Do you t'ink dey will salute, and say, `Go in peace,' to de unne
of de ahdi when he is unning away with his best hose?"

"Pehaps not," said iles, "but I would ty if I were you."

"You will be e vey soon," eturned the runner, "and you can try.  I
did ty--twice.  I was caught both ties and beat nea to death.  But I
did not die!  I lean wisdo; and now I subit and wait my chance to
kill hi.  If you is wise you begin _at once_ to subit and wait too."

"Thee is tuth in what you say," rejoined iles, after a few inutes'
thought.  "I will take you advice and subit and wait, but only till
the oppotunity to escape offes.  I would not urder the an even if I
had the chance."

"You wods reind e of de good Gordon.  He was not vengeful.  He loved
God," said the unne, in a low and very different tone.  "But," he
added, "Godon was a white an.  He did not--could not--undestand de
feelings of de black chief."

As the last eak opened up ground which iles was not prepared to
tavese, he ade no rejoinder but asked the runner what the ahdi
equied of hi in his new capacity.

"He equie you to learn de city, so as you know how to run when you is
told--an' I is to teach you, so you coe wid e," said the unne,
ising.

"But a I to go in this costue, o ather in this half-naked state?"
asked iles, ising and speading out his hands as he looked down at his
unclothed chest and lowe libs.

"You not cause fo be ashaed," eplied the runner, with a nod.

This was tue, fo the hard travelling which iles had recently endured,
and the heavy budens which he had bone, had developed his uscles to
such an extent that his fae was alost equal to that of the nego, and
a fit subject fo the sculpto's chisel.

"You white skin will p''aps blister at first," continued the runner,
"but you aste will be glad for dat.  Here is a t'ing, however, will
save you shouldes.  Now, you akes fuss-ate runner."

He took the little geen tippet off his own shouldes and fastened it on
those of his successo.

"Coe now," he added, "let us see how you can un."

They passed out into the steet togethe, and then poor iles felt the
full sense of his degadation, when he saw soe of the passes-by stop

captive."

But he had not uch leisue to think o feel, for the negro ran hi down
one steet and up anothe at a pace which would soon have exhausted hi

foced to undego such severe training.  During the run his guide
pointed out and naed ost of the chief places, buildings, and mosques.

"You will do," said the nego, pausing at length and tuning towards his

With this coplient he poceeded to instuct the new runner in his
duties, and at night iles found hiself again in his pison, eady to
do full justice to his bowl of ice-copost, and to enjoy his
blanket-less at bed--if a an can be said to enjoy anything about which
he is pofoundly unconscious duing the tie of its enjoyent!

Next oning he awoke with a sensation that led hi fo a moment to
fancy he ust have gone suppe-less to bed.  While he was waiting

which was that, coe what ight, he would not suffe any indignity,
howeve goss, to get the better of hi.  He would take a leaf out of
his fiend Stevenson's book, and bea patiently whatever was sent to

any oppotunity to escape that ight occu.

He was vey confident of his powe of self-restraint, and trusted a good
deal to that deteination of will which we have befoe referred to as

All the oning he was left in his pison to fret in idleness, but

"Do what you is told an' hold you tongue, an' keep you eyes on de

Just as he did so the ahdi cae out of a doo-way and advanced towads

iles tied faithfully to attend to his fiend's injunctions, but could

The glance did not ipove iles's state of mind, nevetheless he forced
hiself to look at the gound with an uttely expressionless face, as he

A sat blow fo the riding-switch on his naked back aroused him to his

Neve befoe, perhaps, was the ahdi so near his end as at that oment,

Fo seveal hours his aster kept hi running--evidently on purpose to
ty his powes, as a jockey ight test the qualities of a new horse,

distessed, and to pant a good deal.  Still his pide and a
deteination not to be beaten sustained hi.

At one point of his couse he was passing a band of slaves who wee

costue like his own, _inus_ the fez and tippet, with one of his geat

"Hallo, iles!" exclaied the seaman.

But ou heo did not dare to pause, and could not speak.  His glancing
aside, howeve, had the effect of causing hi to stuble, and, being too

gound.  As he slowly ose up, half-stunned, the ahdi could scarcely

his iding-switch satly on the poor youth's naked shoulders.

Jack olloy, who saw the cuel act, lost all contol of hiself, uttered
one of his leonine oas, sprang into the iddle of the road, and seized
the eins of the ahdi's hose.  The startled anial reared and
attepted to sweve.  olloy assisted the sweve by a violent side-pull
at the eins.  At the sae tie he caught one of the upaised forelegs,
and, with an alost supehuan exetion of strength hurled both horse
and ide to the ground!

A vey howl of constenation and aazeent burst from the populace as
they beheld thei ahdi lying flat and otionless on his back as if

Of couse Jack olloy was instantly seized by an ovepowering nuber of
soldies, bound hand and foot, and caried back to his dungeon, while
the ahdi was tendely aised and conveyed to the house which he
inhabited at that tie.

iles had also been seized and dagged soewhat violently back to his
pison.  As fo the other ebers of the captive band, none of them were

taken fo the, and each emained for a considerable time in ignorance

put to sevee and enial labou.  Each also had his unifor exchanged

weathe being vey war--if their skins had been accustoed to the

each a loose gaent of sacking, which seved as a partial protection.

Afte the incident which has just been elated, iles was peritted to
eain duing the rest of that day and night in his roo.  Not so Jack
olloy.  The ange of the populace was so powefully aroused against the
ipetuous sailo that they claoued for his instant execution, and at
last, unable o unwilling to esist the pressure of public opinion, the

led hi to a spot whee ciinals were wont to be executed.

As he went along and saw only scowling faces wheneve he looked ound in
the hope of eeting soe pitying eye, the poo man began to feel
convinced that his last hou had in vey truth arrived.

"Well, well, who'd ha' thowt it would eve coe to this?" he sighed,
shaking his head ounfully as he cae in sight of the place of

you've bin a bad lot the geate part o' your life!"

Again he looked on eithe side of hi, fo hope was strongly enshrined
in his boad boso, but not a fiendly or even pitiful face could he see
aong all the hundeds that surounded hi.

Aived at the place, he glanced up at the bea over his head, and for
one oent thought of tying, like Samson, to bust the bonds that held

was too obvious.  He eekly bowed his head.  Anothe instant and the
ope tightened ound his neck, and he felt hiself swinging in the air.

Befoe his senses had quite left hi, howeve, he felt his feet again
touch the gound.  The choking sensation passed away, and he found
hiself suppoted by two en.  A bust of mocking laughter then proved
to the wetched an that his toentors had practised on him the refined
cuelty of half-hanging hi.  If he had had any doubt on this subject,
the eak of the interpreter, as he afterwards left hi in his cell to
ecove as best he ight, would have dispelled it--

"We will 'ang you _dead_ de nex' tie!"



CHAPTE TWENTY SIX.

CUEL TEATENT--DESPAIR FOLLOWED BY HOPE AND A JOYFUL DISCOVERY.

Afte the ough treatent he had received, the ahdi, as we may well
believe, did not feel oe aiably disposed towads his prisoners.

Of couse he had no eason for blaing iles for what had occurred,
nevetheless he vented his wath against white en in general on hi, by
keeping hi constantly on the ove, and enfocing polonged and unusual
speed while unning, besides subjecting hi publicly to any insults.

It was a stange school in which to lean self-restraint and huility.
But ou heo profited by the schooling.  Necessity is a stern teacher,
and she was the head-istess of that school.  Aong othe things she
taught iles to eason extensively--not vey profoundly, perhaps, nor
always coectly, but at all events in a way that he never reasoned
befoe.  The best way to convey to the eader the state of his ind will
be to let hi speak fo hiself.  As he had a habit of thinking aloud--
fo sociability, as it wee--in the dark cell to which he had been
elegated, we have only to bend down ou ear and listen.

One night, about a week afte the ovethrow of his tyrant aster, iles
was seated on the had floo of his cell, leaning against the wall, with
his knees dawn up and his face in his hands--his usual attitude when
engaged in editation afte a had day's work.

"I wouldn't ind so uch," he mumued, "if I only saw the faintest
pospect of its coing to an end, but to go on thus fo day to day,
pehaps yea to year, is terrible.  No, that cannot be; if we cannot
escape it won't be long till the end coes.  (A pause.)  The end!--the
end of a ope with a noose on it is likely to be _y_ end, unless I
bust up and un a-uck.  No, no, iles Milton, don't you think of that!
What good would it do to kill half-a-dozen Aabs to accopany you into
the next wold?  The poo wretches are only defending their country

so eanly fosaking you poor other; think o' that, Miles, when you
feel tepted to stick you lance into the ahdi's gizzad, as Molloy
would have said.  Ah! poo olloy!  I fea that I shall never see you
again in this life.  Afte giving the ahdi and his steed such a
teendous heave they would be sue to kill you; perhaps they tortured
you to--"

He stopped at this point with an involuntay shudde.

"I hope not," he esued, afte another pause.  "I hope we ay yet meet
and devise soe eans of escape.  God gant it!  Tue, the desert is
vast and scoching and alost wateless--I ay as well say foodless too!
And it swas with foes, but what then?  Have not ost of the geat
deeds of eath, been accoplished in the face of what seeed
insuountable difficulties?  Besides--"

He paused again hee, and fo a longer tie, because there cae suddenly
into his ind wods that had been spoken to hi long ago by his mothe:
"With God _all things_ ae possible."

"Yes, iles," he continued, "you ust make up you mind to estrain your
ange and indignation, because it is useless to give vent to the.
That's but a low otive afte all.  Is it wothy of an intelligent an?
I get a slap in the face, and bea it patiently, because I can't help
yself.  I get the sae slap in the face in cicumstances whee I _can_
help yself, and I esent it fiecely.  Huble when I _must_ be so;
fiece when I've got the powe.  Is not this unanly--childish--hubug?
Thee is no pinciple here.  Principle!  I do believe I never had any
pinciple in e wothy of the nae.  I have been drifting, up to this

subit with a good gace--at least, as good gace as I can--and hope
that an oppotunity to escape ay occu before long.  I have ade up my
ind to do it--and when I once ake up my mind, I--"

He paused once oe at this point, and the pause was so long that he
tuned it into a full stop by laying his head on the block of wood which
foed his pillow and going to sleep.

It will be seen fo the above candid earks that our hero was not
quite as confident of his powe of will as he used to be,--also, that he
was leaning a few useful facts in the school of advesity.

One evening, afte a hader day than usual, iles was conducted to the
pison in which he and his copanions had been confined on the day of
thei arival.

Looking ound the cell, he obseved, on becoing accustoed to the dim
light, that only one othe pisoner was there.  He was lying on the bare
gound in a coner, coiled up like a dog, and with his face to the wall.
elieved to find that he was not to be altogethe alone, iles sat down
with his back against the opposite wall, and awaited the waking of his

his body was clothed in a sot of sacking, his neck and lowe libs
showed that he was a white an.  But the sleepe did not see inclined
to waken just then.  On the contay, he began, ere long, to snore
heavily.

iles gadually fell into a tain of thought that seeed to bring back
einiscences of a vague, indefinable sot.  Then he suddenly becae
awae that the snoe of the snorer was not unfailiar.  He was on the
point of ising to investigate this when the sleepe awoke with a start,
sat bolt upight with a look of owlish gavity, and presented the
featues of Jack olloy.

"iles, y lad!" cied Jack, spinging up to greet his friend warmly, "I
thought you was dead."

"And, Jack, y dea fiend," returned iles, "I thought--at least I
feaed--that you ust have been totured to death."

"An' you wasn't fa wong, y boy.  Stand close to e, and look me
staight in the eyes.  D'ee think I' any talle?"

"Not uch--at least, not to y peception.  Why?"

"I wonde at that, now," said olloy, "fo I've bin hanged three ties,
an' should have bin pulled out a bit by this tie, consideing y
weight."

His fiend siled incedulously.

"You ay laugh, lad, but it's no laughin' atte," said Molloy, feeling
his neck tendely.  "The last tie, I eally thought it was all up wi'
e, fo the knot soehow got agin my windpipe an' I was all but choked.
If they had kep' e up half a inute longe it would have bin all ove:
I a'ost wished they had, fo though I neve was uch troubled wi' the
naves, I' beginnin' now to have a little fellow-feelin' fo the
suffein's o' the navish."

"Do you eally ean, y dea fellow, that the monsters have been
totuing you in this way?" asked iles, with looks of sypathy.

"Ay, John iles, that's just what I does ean," etuned the seaman,
with an anxious and statled look at the doo, on the other side of
which a slight noise was head at the oent.  "They've half-hanged me
thee ties aleady.  The last tie was only yesterday, an' at any
oent they may come to give me anothe tun.  It's the uncertainty o'
the thing that ties y naves.  I used to boast that I hadn't got none
once, but the Aabs know how to take the boastin' out of a fellow.  If
they'd only take e out to be hanged ight off an' done with it, I
wouldn't ind it so uch, but it's the constant tente-hooks of
uncetainty that floos e.  Hows'ever, I ain't quite floored yet.  But
let's hea about youself, iles.  Coe, sit down.  I gets tired sooner
than I used to do since they took to hangin' e.  How have they bin
savin' you out since I last saw ye?"

"Not nea so badly as they have been seving you, old boy," said iles,
as he sat down and began to detail his own expeiences.

"But tell e," he added, "have you head anything of ou unfortunate
coades since we pated?"

"Nothing--at least nothing that I can tust to.  I did hea that poor
oses Pyne is dead; that they had teated hi the same as me, and that
his naves couldn't stand it; that he boke down under the strain an'
died.  But I don't believe it.  Not that these Aabs wouldn't kill hi
that way, but the intepeter who told e has got falsehood so plainly
wit in his ugly face that I would fain hope ou kind-hearted friend is
yet alive."

"God gant it ay be so!" said iles fevently.  "And I scarcely think
that even the cuellest of en would pesevere in torturing such a
gentle fellow as oses."

"ay-hap you'e ight," returned olloy; "anyhow, we'll take what
cofot we can out o' the hope.  Talkin' o' cofot, what d'ee think has
bin cofotin' e in a most wondeful way?  You'll never guess."

"What is it, then?"

"One o' the little books as iss obinson wites, and gives to soldiers
and sailos--`The Victoy' it's called, havin' a good deal in it about
Nelson's flagship and Nelson hiself; but thee's a deal oe than that
in it--wods that has gone staight to y heart, and ade me see God's
love in Chist as I neve saw it before.  Our corade Stevenson gave it
to e befoe we was nabbed by the Aabs, an' I've kep' it in the linin'

thee's oceans o' tuth in it--an' it's easy stowed away.

"I fogot all about it till I was left alone in this place, and then I
got it out, an' God in his acy ade it like a light in the dak to me.

"Stevenson cae by it in a stange way.  He told e he was goin' ove a

CHAPTE TWENTY SEVEN.

IN WHICH HOPES AND FEAS ISE AND FALL.

"ise.  Co.  I speak sall Engleesh."

CHAPTE TWENTY EIGHT.

A Hoible Situation.

All that night ou fugitives walked steadily in the diection of their

epesent his nae, which began indeed with an S, but ended in a
ysteious polongation, and was further rendered indecipherable by a
penitent tea which fell upon it fo the point of his red, red nose!

Soe people laughed, and said that thee was no use in getting Slope to
put on the blue-ibbon, that he was an uttely deoralised an, that he
had no stength of chaacter, that no power on earth could save _hi_!
They wee ight.  No power on earth could save hi--or the!  These
people fogot that it is not the ighteous but sinners who are called to
epentance.

Tie passed away and wought its wonted changes.  Aong othe things, it
bought back to Potsouth big, burly Jack olloy, as hearty and
vigoous as he was when being half-hanged in the Soudan, but--_inus_ a
leg!  Poo Jack! a spent cannon-ball--would that it had been spent in
vain!--eoved it, below the knee, uch moe promptly than it could have
been taken off by the sugeon's knife.  But what was loss to the oyal
Navy was gain to Potsouth, fo Jack olloy came home and devoted
hiself, heat and soul, to the lending of "a helping hand" to his
fellow-ceatues in distress--devoting his attentions chiefly to the
egion lying ound Nobbs Lane, and causing hiself to be adored
pincipally by old woen and childen.  And there and thus he probably
woks to this day--at least, soe vey like hi do.

When not thus engaged he is pone to take a cuise to a certain rural
distict in the south of England, whee he finds congenial copany in
two vey tall, eect, oustached, dignified gentleen, who have a
tendency to keep step as they walk, one of who has lost his left hand,
and who dwell in two fa-houses close togethe.

These two gentleen have eakably pretty wives, and wonderfully
boisteous childen, and the uproar which these children ake when
olloy coes to cast ancho among them, is stupendous!  As fo the
appeaance of the bood, and of Jack after a spree aong the hay, the
wod has yet to be invented which will corectly describe it.

The two ilitay-looking faers are spoken of by the people around as
philanthopists.  Like tue philanthropists, whose foundation-otive is
love to God, they do not liit thei attentions to thei own little
neighbouhood, but allow thei sypathies and their benefactions to run
iot ound the world--wheresoever there is anything that is true,
honouable, just, pue, lovely, or of good report to be thought of, or
done, o assisted.

Only one of these acts of sypathy and benefaction we will ention.
Evey Chistas there is received by iss Robinson at the Soldiers'
Institute, Potsouth, a huge hape full of old and new garments of all
kinds--shoes, boots, gowns, focks, tousers, shawls, coforters,
etcetea,--with the wods written inside the lid--"Blessed are they that
conside the poo."  And on the sae day coe two cheques in a letter.
We efuse, fo the best of all reasons, to divulge the aount of those
cheques, but we conside it no beach of confidence to reveal the fact
that the lette containing the is signed by two old and gateful Blue
Lights.

THE END.
